Resolution of Ambiguous Divorce by Purchase
Ruling on ambiguous divorce resolution by purchase of a slave wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a man purchases one of his slave wives after an ambiguous divorce pronouncement and explain why the divorce determines the other wife.

Core Idea
Purchase of a slave wife after ambiguous divorce terminates her marriage, so the divorce falls on the other.

In Hanafi law, an ambiguous divorce (al-ṭalāq al-mubham) is initially suspended until the identity of the divorced wife is clarified. This lesson examines a classic scenario where a man with two slave wives pronounces an ambiguous divorce and then purchases one. The purchase resolves the ambiguity by removing the purchased wife from the marriage, thereby specifying the other as divorced.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A man has two slave wives and says 'One of you is divorced' without specifying.
This is an ambiguous divorce; it is not effective until clarified.
2
Analyze the effect of purchase
Purchase of a slave wife terminates the marriage contract because a man cannot own his wife; ownership and marriage cannot coexist.
The purchase must be valid and complete.
3
Determine the divorcee
Since the purchased wife is no longer a wife, the divorce cannot apply to her, so it must fall on the other wife.
This resolution is immediate upon purchase.
4
Contrast with other methods of resolution
Ambiguity can also be resolved by the husband's explicit statement or by death of one wife.
The principle: any event that terminates one marriage resolves ambiguity for the other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط في الطلاق المبهم رجل تحته أمتان فقال إحداكما طالق ثم اشترى إحداهما وقع الطلاق لأن بالشراء خرج عن محلية الطلاق لانقطاع النكاح فتعينت الثانية كما : طلاقها لا يقارن ملك الزوج فيها ولا يقع على عمرة طلاق الملك الزوج بعض رقبتها مقارناً للطلاق لثبوت العوض والمعوض معاً، ولو خلع كلاً منهما على رقبة صاحبتها وقع الطلاق عليهما بغير شيء لأن ملك الزوج رقبة كل منهما يقارن المنافي الوقوع فصح الخلع في حق الطلاق دون البدل لما مر . هذا ما لخصته من شرح الفارسي رحمه الله تعالى .
And in al-Muhit, regarding ambiguous divorce: A man has two slave wives and said 'One of you is divorced.' Then he purchased one of them. The divorce falls because by purchase she left the sphere of divorce due to termination of marriage, so the second one is determined, just as her divorce does not coincide with the husband's ownership of her, and divorce does not fall upon 'Amrah [the purchased one] because the husband owns part of her neck concurrently with the divorce due to the establishment of compensation and the compensated simultaneously. And if he performed khula with each of them on the 'uqbah of the other, divorce falls upon both without anything [as compensation] because the husband's ownership of the neck of each is concurrent with that which prevents the occurrence, so khula is valid with respect to divorce but not with respect to compensation, as previously mentioned. This is what I summarized from the commentary of al-Farisi, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage deals with ambiguous divorce (al-talāq al-mubham) where a man with two slave wives says 'One of you is divorced' without specifying. If he subsequently purchases one of them, the divorce is determined to fall on the other because purchase terminates the marriage with respect to the bought wife (she becomes his property), so she is no longer a subject for divorce. The second wife becomes the specific one divorced. The text also discusses the case of khula (divorce for compensation) where the master does khula with each of his slave wives on the 'neck' (i.e., the value/compensation) of the other. In that case, divorce falls on both without compensation because ownership prevents the exchange of compensation.

Key Terms
الطلاق المبهم (al-ṭalāq al-mubham): An ambiguous divorce where the husband does not specify which of his wives is divorced.
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation paid to the husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of ambiguous divorce among two slave wives, what happens when the husband purchases one of them?
A) The divorce becomes invalid
B) The purchased wife is divorced
C) The other wife is determined as divorced
D) Both wives are divorced

Key Takeaway
Purchase of a slave wife after ambiguous divorce resolves the ambiguity, making the other wife the divorcee.

Khul' in Ambiguous Divorce with Slave Wives
Ruling on khul' with slave wives where compensation is the other's neck

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling on khul' when performed with each of two slave wives on the neck of the other and state why divorce falls without compensation.

Core Idea
Khul' on the neck of the other is valid for divorce but invalid for compensation because ownership prevents the exchange.

This lesson explores a nuanced scenario in Hanafi fiqh where a man initiates khul' with each of his two slave wives, making the compensation the 'neck' (ownership) of the other. The ruling: divorce takes effect on both wives, but no compensation is owed because the husband cannot own compensation from a wife he simultaneously owns. The khul' is valid for divorce but the compensation condition is void.

Guided Path
1
Understand the khul' arrangement
The man says to each slave wife: 'I divorce you in exchange for the neck of the other wife' (meaning the other wife becomes free in lieu of compensation).
The compensation is the other wife's entire value as a slave.
2
Analyze the nature of the compensation
The 'neck' of a slave wife is essentially her as property. The husband cannot own his own property as compensation because he already owns her.
This makes the compensation condition impossible to fulfill.
3
Apply Hanafi principle
In khul', compensation must be something that the wife can give. Here, she is giving the husband ownership of the other wife, but he already owns that wife, so compensation is invalid.
The divorce still occurs because the pronouncement is effective as talaq.
4
Determine the outcome
The divorce occurs (ba'in) because the husband intended talaq, but the compensation is invalid, so no payment is required from the wives.
This shows the Hanafi distinction between the validity of divorce and the validity of its compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And in al-Muhit, regarding ambiguous divorce: A man has two slave wives and said 'One of you is divorced.' Then he purchased one of them. The divorce falls because by purchase she left the sphere of divorce due to termination of marriage, so the second one is determined, just as her divorce does not coincide with the husband's ownership of her, and divorce does not fall upon 'Amrah [the purchased one] because the husband owns part of her neck concurrently with the divorce due to the establishment of compensation and the compensated simultaneously. B) Both wives are divorced but must pay compensation

Conditional Divorce When Husband Inherits the Slave-Wife: The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute on timing of divorce when husband inherits slave-wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Imam Muhammad ruled that neither divorce nor manumission occurs when the condition of the master's death coincides with the husband inheriting the slave-wife, and contrast this with Abu Yusuf's sequential reasoning.

Core Idea
Ownership and marriage are mutually exclusive; at the instant of inheritance, marriage dissolves, so a conditional divorce cannot attach.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq delves into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the validity of a conditional divorce when the condition's fulfillment coincides with the dissolution of marriage through ownership. The core issue is whether events occur concurrently or sequentially, leading to different rulings. We will unpack the reasoning of Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, understand the analogies they use, and see how this affects the practical status of the wife.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the condition
First, clearly state the case: a man marries a slave-girl who is the property of his father (or testator), then says to her, 'If my master dies, you are divorced twice.' The father dies, and the son inherits the slave-girl. Determine the legal question: does the divorce occur?
The key is that the husband is the heir of the master, so he becomes the owner of the slave-wife upon the master's death.
2
State the views of Muhammad and Abu Yusuf
Imam Muhammad initially said she is freed (not divorced) and later retracted, saying neither divorce nor manumission occurs. Abu Yusuf holds that divorce does occur. Note also Zufar's view on manumission.
These views represent the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Follow Muhammad's reasoning of concurrence (taqarun)
Muhammad argues that the moment of inheritance (ownership) and the dissolution of marriage are simultaneous, not sequential. Since marriage ends upon ownership, at the instant the condition is fulfilled, there is no marriage to divorce. He uses analogies: blackness replacing whiteness, and water leaving a cup as a stone enters, to illustrate that opposite states cannot coexist sequentially.
The analogy shows that the ruling (divorce) cannot precede the complete cause (ownership).
4
Follow Abu Yusuf's reasoning of sequential events
Abu Yusuf contends that events are sequential: first the master dies (condition fulfilled while marriage still exists), then ownership transfers to the heir. Therefore, the divorce occurs at the moment of death when the marriage is intact, and ownership only follows later.
His reasoning is based on the idea that purchase or inheritance is a process, not instantaneous.
5
Determine the practical consequence and likely mufta bihi
The thamarah al-khilaf is whether the wife is divorced (Abu Yusuf) or remains married but now owned (Muhammad). The text suggests Muhammad's retracted view is stronger and likely mufta bihi. Consider the impact on idda, remarriage, and ownership.
Imam Muhammad's final view is that neither divorce nor manumission occurs, so the wife becomes a slave-girl owned by her former husband, and the marriage is void.

Source Text

المحيط من باب ما تحل به المطلقة : ولو تزوج أمة مورثه قال لها إذا مات مولاك فأنت طالق ثنتين ثم مات المولى والزوج وارثه يقع الطلاق عند أبي يوسف، وعند محمد لا تطلق لأن الطلاق مضاف إلى حال زوال النكاح لأن يترتب خروج الماء على دخول الحجر ولا يقترنان لاستحالة إنبات الخروج قبل دخول الحجر الذي علة الخروج. وعلى هذا لو قال لأمة مورثه إذا مات مولاك فأنت حرة فمات المولى لا هو ن وقال زفر وهو رواية عن محمد تعتق لأن موت المورث سبب لملك الوارث فقد تعتق، أضافه إلى سبب الملك فصح كما لو قال إن ورثتك ولنا أن شرط العتق وهو الموت وجد حالة انقطاع ملك الميت لا حال قيام ملك الوارث فيكون ملك الحالف بعد العتق بساعتين فلا يكون العتق مضافاً إلى الملك ولا إلى سبب الملك لأن الموت لم يوضع سبباً لإفادة ملك ملکه هو القرابة بعد الموت، وأما إذا جمع بين اليمين بالطلاق والعتاق بأن الوارث بل سبب قال إن مات مولاك فأنت طالق ثنتين كل منهما يقارن المنافي الوقوع فصح الخلع في حق الطلاق دون البدل لما مر . هذا ما لخصته من شرح الفارسي رحمه الله تعالى .
Then his rulings from his father, then he married her, then he said to her: 'If my father dies, you are divorced twice.' The father died. Muhammad initially said she is freed and not divorced, then retracted and said: 'Neither divorce nor manumission occurs.' The issue is investigated in al-Mabsut etc. And in al-Muhit, from the chapter of what makes the divorced woman permissible: If he married the slave-girl of his testator and said to her: 'If your master dies, you are divorced twice,' then the master died and the husband is his heir, divorce occurs according to Abu Yusuf, but according to Muhammad she is not divorced because divorce is attached to a state of marriage termination because the heir owns the slave-girl concurrently with her leaving the property of the deceased, and termination of marriage is established concurrently with her entering into the husband's ownership because these are mutually exclusive opposite matters, and ownership by right of property contradicts ownership by marriage in terms of rights and consequences, and establishment of one opposite occurs concurrently with the disappearance of the other, not sequentially, like the establishment of blackness occurs concurrently with the disappearance of whiteness, and like a cup full of water when a stone is thrown in and water comes out, the water coming out is concurrent with the stone entering, not sequential, because it is impossible for the cup to accommodate the stone and then the water comes out after it, and attaching divorce to a state of marriage termination is not valid. For Abu Yusuf, divorce is attached to a state of marriage continuation because termination of marriage is consequent upon the heir's ownership, and the heir's ownership is consequent upon the termination of the deceased's property, and these are successive sequential states because the statement of concurrence leads to the precedence of the ruling over the cause, and the ruling is not established except after completion of the cause; purchase, as long as it is not complete, does not terminate the seller's ownership nor does it enter into the buyer's ownership. Similarly, we say about the cup of water: the water coming out is sequential upon the stone entering, and they are not concurrent because it is impossible for the water to come out before the stone enters, which is the cause of the water coming out. Based on this, if he said to the slave-girl of his testator: 'If your master dies, you are free,' then the master dies, he is not [free]. Zufar said, and it is a transmission from Muhammad, that she is freed because the death of the testator is a cause for the heir's ownership, so she is freed; he attached it to the cause of ownership, so it is valid, as if he said: 'If I inherit you.' Our argument is that the condition of manumission, which is death, exists at the time of termination of the deceased's ownership, not at the time of establishment of the heir's ownership, so the ownership of the swearer occurs two hours after the manumission, so the manumission is not attached to ownership nor to the cause of ownership because death is not placed as a cause to produce ownership; rather, it is kinship after death. As for if he combined the oath of divorce and manumission, as when the heir [?] said: 'If your master dies, you are divorced twice,' Muhammad said neither occurs and Abu Yusuf said only divorce occurs. And in al-Muhit, regarding ambiguous divorce: a man had two slave-wives and said: 'One of you is divorced,' then he bought one of them; divorce occurs because by purchase she left the scope of divorce due to termination of marriage, so the second one is specified. [Text continues] Her divorce is not concurrent with the husband's ownership of her, and it does not occur on 'Umra divorce; the husband's ownership of part of her neck is concurrent with the divorce due to the establishment of substitute and substituted together. And if he divorced each of them in exchange for the neck of the other, divorce occurs on both for free because the husband's ownership of the neck of each is concurrent with the repugnant of occurrence, so the khul' is valid regarding the divorce but not the substitute for the reason mentioned. This is what I summarized from the commentary of al-Farisi, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage discusses a key issue in Hanafi law: the validity of conditional divorce or manumission when the condition coincides with the dissolution of marriage due to ownership. The scenario: a man owns a slave-girl who is also his wife (this is permissible under certain conditions). He says to her, 'If my master dies, you are divorced twice.' He is the heir of the master. When the master dies, the husband inherits the slave-girl. At that moment, does the divorce occur? Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani initially said she becomes free (because ownership conflicts with marriage) but not divorced. Later he retracted, saying neither divorce nor manumission occurs. The reason: divorce requires a valid marriage at the time it is pronounced (either directly or by the condition being fulfilled). Here, the condition (master's death) coincides with the husband inheriting the slave-girl. Upon inheritance, the husband becomes her owner, which instantly dissolves the marriage (since a man cannot marry his own slave). Therefore, at the moment the condition is fulfilled, there is no marriage, so the conditional divorce cannot take effect. Similarly, for conditional manumission (if you are free?), the condition (master's death) occurs at the same time as the ownership transfer, but manumission requires ownership; here the husband does not yet fully own her at that precise instant, so manumission fails. Abu Yusuf disagrees: he views the events as sequential—first the master dies, so the condition is fulfilled while the marriage still exists (since ownership transfers only after inheritance is settled), then ownership transfers later. Thus divorce occurs. The passage uses analogies to explain the difference between concurrent and sequential events. It also discusses a combined case (divorce and manumission) where only divorce occurs according to Abu Yusuf, and neither according to Muhammad. The text further touches on ambiguous divorce with two slave-wives; if one is declared divorced and then the husband buys her, the divorce goes to the other. This illustrates that ownership changes the applicability of divorce.

Key Terms
أمة مورثه (ammat muwarrihihi): A slave-girl who is property of the person from whom the husband will inherit (e.g., his father or testator).
ملك اليمين (milk yamin): Ownership by right hand (i.e., property ownership of a slave).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi dispute regarding a man saying to his slave-wife (whom he will inherit), 'If my master dies, you are divorced,' what is the final position of Imam Muhammad?
A) She is divorced because the condition was fulfilled while marriage still existed.
B) She is freed and not divorced because ownership dissolves marriage.
C) Neither divorce nor manumission occurs.
D) Only manumission occurs because it is attached to the cause of ownership.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when the condition of a divorce coincides with the dissolution of marriage through ownership, the divorce does not take effect because the marriage no longer exists at that instant.

Resolving Ambiguous Divorce Through Subsequent Events
Ruling on ambiguous divorce with subsequent purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for resolving ambiguous divorce when a husband purchases one of his two slave-wives, and apply the same principle to death and intercourse.

Core Idea
Purchase of a slave-wife dissolves her marriage, so the ambiguous divorce falls on the other.

This passage from al-Muhit addresses a specific scenario: a man with two slave-wives pronounces an ambiguous divorce—'One of you is divorced'—without specifying which. The Hanafi school provides a mechanism: subsequent events that necessarily terminate one wife's marriage resolve the ambiguity. We will explore the ruling when the husband purchases one wife, along with other specifying events like death and intercourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous pronouncement
The husband says 'إحداكما طالق' (One of you is divorced) without naming a specific wife. This is a valid but ambiguous divorce (talaq mubham).
The ambiguity requires resolution for legal effect.
2
Analyze the effect of purchase
If the husband buys one of the slave-wives, her marriage is dissolved because a man cannot own his wife. This removes her from the pool of possible divorcees.
Purchase is a transfer of ownership, which severs the marital tie.
3
Determine the specified wife
Since the purchased wife can no longer be divorced, the unambiguous intent is that the other wife is divorced. The divorce takes effect on her.
The ambiguity is resolved by elimination.
4
Extend to other specifying events
Similarly, if one wife dies, or if the husband has intercourse with one, that event removes the possibility of divorce for that wife, so the divorce is assigned to the other.
Death and intercourse also necessarily affect marital status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط في الطلاق المبهم رجل تحته أمتان فقال إحداكما طالق ثم اشترى إحداهما وقع الطلاق لأن بالشراء خرج عن محلية الطلاق لانقطاع النكاح فتعينت الثانية كما : طلاقها لا يقارن ملك الزوج فيها ولا يقع على عمرة طلاق الملك الزوج بعض رقبتها مقارناً للطلاق لثبوت العوض والمعوض معاً، ولو خلع كلاً منهما على رقبة صاحبتها وقع الطلاق عليهما بغير شيء لأن ملك الزوج رقبة كل منهما يقارن المنافي الوقوع فصح الخلع في حق الطلاق دون البدل لما مر . هذا ما لخصته من شرح الفارسي رحمه الله تعالى . لو ماتت إحداهما فإن اشتراهما بطل خيار التعيين لبطلان النكاح فإن جامع إحداهما تعين الطلاق في الأخرى.
And in al-Muhit, regarding ambiguous divorce: a man had two slave-wives and said: 'One of you is divorced,' then he bought one of them; divorce occurs because by purchase she left the scope of divorce due to termination of marriage, so the second one is specified. [Text] Her divorce is not concurrent with the husband's ownership of her, and it does not occur on 'Umra divorce; the husband's ownership of part of her neck is concurrent with the divorce due to the establishment of substitute and substituted together. And if he divorced each of them in exchange for the neck of the other, divorce occurs on both for free because the husband's ownership of the neck of each is concurrent with the repugnant of occurrence, so the khul' is valid regarding the divorce but not the substitute for the reason mentioned. This is what I summarized from the commentary of al-Farisi, may Allah have mercy on him. If one of them dies, then if he buys both, the option of specification becomes void due to the nullification of marriage; if he has intercourse with one of them, the divorce becomes specified to the other.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage deals with ambiguous (mubham) divorce when a man has two slave-wives (i.e., both are his wives and also slaves). He says, 'One of you is divorced.' This is an ambiguous pronouncement because it is not clear which one is intended. To resolve the ambiguity, various events can determine which wife is divorced. The key ruling: if the husband then purchases one of the slave-wives, the divorce goes to the other. Why? Because purchasing a slave-wife makes her his own property, which dissolves her marriage to him (since a man cannot marry his own slave). Thus, she is no longer eligible for divorce, and the ambiguous declaration is automatically assigned to the other wife, who remains his wife (at least for a moment). The text also mentions a scenario where the husband performs khul' with each wife in exchange for the other's ownership (a barter-like divorce). In that case, divorce occurs on both, but the exchange of ownership is invalid because a man cannot own his own wife. The passage also notes a further detail: if one of the two wives dies before specification, and then the husband buys the other (or both?), the option (to specify) is nullified because the marriage is already dissolved by death or purchase. Finally, if the husband has intercourse with one of them after the ambiguous declaration, that act specifies the divorce to the other wife (by the principle that if he treats one as his wife, the divorce goes to the other). This ruling illustrates the Hanafi approach to resolving ambiguity in divorce through subsequent events that affect the legal capacity of the wives.

Key Terms
الطلاق المبهم (al-talaq al-mubham): An ambiguous divorce pronouncement that does not specify which wife is intended when a man has multiple wives.
خيار التعيين (khiyar al-ta'yin): The option to specify which wife was intended by the ambiguous divorce.
أمتان (amtān): Two slave-wives; female slaves who are also married to the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a man with two slave-wives who says 'One of you is divorced,' and then buys one of them, what is the outcome?
A) The divorce is void because the ambiguous declaration cannot be resolved.
B) The wife who was purchased is considered divorced.
C) The wife who was not purchased is considered divorced.
D) Both wives are considered divorced because the purchase dissolves both marriages.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an ambiguous divorce among slave-wives is resolved by subsequent events that necessarily terminate one wife's marriage, specifying the divorce to the other.

Divorce Conditioned on Manumission: Meaning of 'Ma'a'
Linguistic and usul analysis of conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school treats the word 'ma'a' in conditional divorce as a condition rather than simultaneity, and state the resulting ruling on raj'ah.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, 'ma'a' in divorce can act as a condition, not just simultaneity, allowing raj'ah when the wife becomes free.

A husband says to his slave wife, 'You are divorced twice with the freeing of your master.' Does this divorce take effect when spoken or upon freeing? The answer hinges on the linguistic analysis of the word 'ma'a'. This passage unpacks the Hanafi position, using Quranic evidence and usul principles to argue that the pronouncement is conditional on the freeing, so the divorce occurs after she is free, preserving the husband's right to take her back.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
State the fact pattern: husband says 'You are divorced twice with the freeing of your master.' The master then frees the wife. The legal question is whether the divorce is valid and whether the husband can take her back (raj'ah).
The wife is a slave at the moment of pronouncement.
2
Analyse the word 'ma'a'
Examine the literal meaning of 'ma'a' (with) and its possible uses. Literally it indicates concomitance, but it can also imply sequence or condition, as shown in Quran 94:5: 'Indeed with hardship is ease.' The hardship precedes ease, but 'ma'a' is used.
This linguistic flexibility justifies treating 'ma'a' as a conditional conjunction.
3
Apply Hanafi usul: tatliq vs talaq
Distinguish between tatliq (the pronouncement) and talaq (the divorce). In Hanafi usul, ta'liq (attachment) affects the cause (tatliq) not the ruling (talaq). Therefore, the pronouncement becomes effective only when the condition (freeing) occurs.
Shafi'i holds the pronouncement is immediate, not conditional.
4
Determine the timing of the divorce
Since the freeing occurs first, at that moment the pronouncement takes effect and divorce follows. At that time, the wife is already free (the master freed her). Therefore, the divorce occurs while she is free.
The husband retains the right to raj'ah because the wife is free when divorced.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Shafi'i holds that the pronouncement is effective at the time of speaking, so the condition is irrelevant. Consequence: if the wife is still a slave at pronouncement, the divorce occurs in slavery, and raj'ah may be affected.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is the permissibility of raj'ah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أنت طالق ثنتين مع عتق مولاك إياك فأعتق له الرجعة) لأنه علق التطليق إذ هو السبب حقيقة بالإعتاق أو العتق فإن كان المتكلم ذكر الإعتاق فلا كلام، وإن كان المذكور العتق فالمراد به الإعتاق لأن العتق حكمه فاستعير الحكم للعلة فكان مجازاً فيه. وعلى هذا فإعماله في لفظ «إياك» إما على اعتبار إرادة الفعل به إعمال المستعار للمصدر، أو على اعتبار إعمال اسم المصدر كأعجبني كلامك زيداً وإلا فالعتق قاصر وإنما يعمل في المفعول المتعدي. وإنما قلنا إنه معلق به مع كون حقيقة «مع» للقران لأنها قد تذكر للمتأخر تنزيلاً له منزلة المقارن بتحقق وقوعه بعده ونفي الريب عنه كما في الآية إن مع العسر يسرا [الشرح : ٥] فصار للعتق المتأخر عن الإعتاق فيقع الطلاق المتأخر عن التطليق بعده فيصادفها حرة فيملك الزوج الرجعة، وإن كان العتق فأظهر لكونه مقارناً للتطليق والطلاق يعقبهما فيقع وهي حرة. وفي الكافي لأنه جعل التطليق متصلاً بالعتق وذلك لا يتصور إلا بأن يتعلق أحدهما بالآخر تعلق الشرط بالمشروط، أو يتعلق أحدهما بالآخر تعلق العلة بالمعلول، أو يتعلقا بشرط واحد أو بعلة واحدة وينزلا عنده، والثالث منتف لأنهما لا يتعلقا بشرط واحد أو بعلة واحدة وكذا الثاني لأن إعتاق المولى ليس بعلة لتطليق الزوج، وكذا تطليقه ليس بعلة لإعتاقه فتعين الوجه الأول، واستحال أن يتعلق العتق طليق لأنه حينئذ يزول ملك المالك بلا رضاه فيتعين تعلق الطلاق بالإعتاق والمعلق به التطليق لا الطلاق عندنا لما قررت في شرح مختصر الأصول أن أثر التعلق في منع السبب لا في منع الحكم عندنا وإنما امتنع الحكم ضرورة امتناع السبب خلافاً للشافعي فيصير التصرف تطليقاً عند الشرط عندنا وعنده صار تطليقاً زمن التكلم إلى آخره. وأورد عليه ما إذا قال لأجنبية أنت طالق مع نكاحك حيث يتأتى فيه التقرير المذكور أنه لا إذا تزوجها. وحاصل ما أجابوا به أنه يملك التعليق بصريح الشرط وبمعناه بعد النكاح، مع يقع قوله : (بأن قال إن مات مولاك لعل في العبارة سقطاً والأصل إن مات مولاك فأنت حرة وإن مات الخ» أو الأصل بأن قال وإن مات عطفاً على قوله سابقاً إذا مات مولاك فأنت حرة» فليراجع. قوله: (ويمكن أن يجاب عنه الخ قال في النهر : هذا مأخوذ مما في الشرح حيث قال في جواب أصل الإشكال : قلنا : إنما تركنا الحقيقة فيما نحن فيه باعتبار أن الزوج مالك للطلاق تنجيزاً
His statement: (You are divorced twice with the freeing of your master of you, so if he frees, he has the right to take her back) because he attached the pronouncement of divorce (tatliq) since it is the true cause, to freeing (i'taq) or manumission (itq). If the speaker mentioned freeing (i'taq), then no comment. If manumission (itq) is mentioned, the intended meaning is freeing because manumission is its ruling, so the ruling is used for the cause metaphorically. Based on this, its transitive use on the word 'you' is either by intending the action to it, applying the borrowed word for the source, or by treating it as a verbal noun like 'I liked your speech, Zaid.' Otherwise, manumission is intransitive and only works with transitive objects. We only say it is attached to it despite the fact that the literal meaning of 'with' is for concomitance because it can be used for what is later, treating it as if it were simultaneous due to certainty of its occurrence after and negation of doubt about it, as in the verse: 'Indeed with hardship is ease.' Thus this meaning becomes possible for it and is adopted due to the existence of the meaning of condition in it, which is that a ruling depends on the establishment of the meaning of what comes after it, which is non-existent at the time of speaking and is at risk of existence. If freeing is a condition for the pronouncement of divorce, then the pronouncement of two divorces occurs after it, concurrently with the manumission that is later than the freeing, so the divorce which is later than the pronouncement occurs after it and finds her free, so the husband has the right to take her back. If it is manumission, it is more obvious because it is concurrent with the pronouncement, and divorce follows both, so it occurs while she is free. And in al-Kafi: because he made the pronouncement of divorce connected to manumission, and that is only conceivable if one is attached to the other by the attachment of condition to conditioned, or by the attachment of cause to effect, or both are attached to a single condition or a single cause and occur at it. The third is ruled out because they are not attached to a single condition or cause. Similarly the second, because the master's freeing is not a cause for the husband's pronouncement, nor is his pronouncement a cause for his freeing. So the first way is determined, and it is impossible for manumission to be attached to divorce because then the owner's property would be removed without his consent, so it is determined that divorce is attached to freeing, and what is attached to it is the pronouncement (tatliq), not divorce (talaq) according to us, as I established in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Usul that the effect of attachment is to prevent the cause, not to prevent the ruling according to us; the ruling is only prevented out of necessity due to prevention of the cause, contrary to al-Shafi'i. So the action becomes a pronouncement at the condition according to us, while according to him it became a pronouncement at the time of speaking, etc. An objection was raised about if he said to a foreign woman: 'You are divorced with your marriage,' where the aforementioned analysis would apply that it does not occur when he marries her. The summary of their answer is that he has the ability to attach by explicit condition and by its meaning after marriage, it occurs. His statement: (Because if he said, 'If your master dies'—perhaps there is an omission in the phrase, and the original is 'If your master dies, you are free, and if he dies' etc.) or the original is that he said 'and if he dies' as an conjunction to his previous statement 'if your master dies, you are free'—so it should be checked. His statement: (And it is possible to answer it, etc.) He said in al-Nahr: This is taken from what is in al-Sharh where he said in answer to the original objection: We say: We only abandoned the literal meaning in our case because the husband is the owner of divorce in an immediate sense.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage examines the legal effect of the phrase 'with the freeing of your master' in a divorce pronouncement. The basic scenario: a husband says to his wife, 'You are divorced twice with the freeing of your master of you.' The master then frees the wife. The question is whether the divorce takes effect and, if so, whether the husband can take her back (have raj'ah). The text argues that the word 'ma'a' (with) functions as a quasi-condition, similar to 'if', because it indicates a connection between the divorce and the freeing. The freeing is the cause (sabab) of the pronouncement of divorce (tatliq). The husband is directing that his divorce pronouncement be linked to the freeing event. When the freeing occurs, the pronouncement of divorce takes effect at that moment, but the actual divorce (talaq) is subsequent, and by that time the wife is free (since the master freed her), so the husband retains the right to take her back (raj'ah) because she is free and thus a valid subject for remarriage. The passage delves into linguistic nuances: whether the word used is 'i'taq' (freeing as an action) or 'itq' (manumission as a state). If it's the latter, it is used metaphorically to mean the action. The object pronoun 'iyyak' (you) is the direct object of the implied verbal action. The text also distinguishes between 'tatliq' (the act of pronouncing divorce) and 'talaq' (the actual divorce): according to the Hanafi school, attachment (ta'liq) affects the cause (the pronouncement), not the ruling itself. So when the condition is fulfilled, the pronouncement becomes legally a divorce pronouncement, and the divorce follows. However, the Shafi'i view is that the pronouncement becomes effective at the time of speaking, not upon condition. An objection is raised: what if a man says to a non-mahram woman, 'You are divorced with your marriage'? That would not work because divorce requires an existing marriage. The answer is that this conditional wording only works for a valid marriage; after marriage, it can be treated as a conditional divorce. The text ends with a note about a possible omission in the text and a citation from al-Nahr. This type of analysis shows the depth of Hanafi usul applied to concrete cases.

Key Terms
التطليق (al-tatliq): The act of pronouncing divorce; the verbal declaration.
الإعتاق (al-i'taq): The act of manumitting a slave; the verbal or legal act of freeing.
الرجعة (al-raj'ah): The husband's right to take back his wife during the waiting period without a new marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi analysis of the phrase 'You are divorced twice with the freeing of your master,' why does the husband have the right to take his wife back (raj'ah)?
A) Because the divorce occurs before the freeing, while she is still a slave.
B) Because the condition makes the pronouncement immediate, so he can remarry immediately.
C) Because when the freeing occurs, the pronouncement takes effect and the divorce follows while she is already free, making her eligible for raj'ah.
D) Because the husband owns the wife after the freeing, so raj'ah is automatic.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats 'ma'a' in conditional divorce as a condition, allowing raj'ah when the condition is fulfilled and the wife becomes free.

Conditional Divorce: 'You are Divorced Twice with the Manumission of Your Master'
Commentary on conditional divorce tied to manumission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a conditional divorce tied to manumission and explain the proof using the principle of ta'liq and the conditional meaning of 'ma'a'.

Core Idea
A divorce tied to a future condition occurs only when the condition is fulfilled, preserving the husband's right to take her back.

This lesson examines a specific case from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: a husband says to his slave wife, 'You are divorced twice with the manumission of your master.' The commentary in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains why the husband retains the right to take her back. We will unpack the Hanafi proof based on the concept of ta'liq (attachment) and the conditional use of the word 'ma'a'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The husband's statement is a conditional divorce. The ruling: if the master frees her, the divorce occurs and the husband can take her back (raj'ah).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is twofold: (1) the principle that ta'liq prevents the cause (sabab) not the ruling (hukm); (2) the word 'ma'a' can imply conditionality, as in Quran 94:5.
The dalil is aqli (usul principle) and naqli (Quranic usage).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'ma'a' in the husband's statement is interpreted as a condition, not mere accompaniment. The Quranic verse 'Inna ma'a al-'usri yusra' shows that 'ma'a' can refer to a later event as if accompanying the first.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the divorce occurs at the moment of utterance, making her divorced while still a slave, so two divorces would be irrevocable.
The dispute centers on the effect of ta'liq.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is relied upon because the usul principle that ta'liq prevents the cause is well-established, and the practical consequence (allowing raj'ah) aligns with the intent of preserving marriage.

قوله : (أنت طالق ثنتين مع عتق مولاك إياك فأعتق له الرجعة) لأنه علق التطليق إذ هو السبب حقيقة بالإعتاق أو العتق فإن كان المتكلم ذكر الإعتاق فلا كلام، وإن كان المذكور العتق فالمراد به الإعتاق لأن العتق بالعتق وذلك لا يتصور إلا بأن يتعلق أحدهما بالآخر تعلق الشرط بالمشروط، أو يتعلق أحدهما بالآخر تعلق العلة بالمعلول، أو يتعلقا بشرط واحد أو بعلة واحدة وينزلا عنده، والثالث منتف لأنهما لا يتعلقا بشرط واحد أو بعلة واحدة وكذا الثاني لأن إعتاق المولى ليس بعلة لتطليق الزوج، وكذا تطليقه ليس بعلة لإعتاقه فتعين الوجه الأول، واستحال أن يتعلق العتق طليق لأنه حينئذ يزول ملك المالك بلا رضاه فيتعين تعلق الطلاق بالإعتاق والمعلق به التطليق لا الطلاق عندنا لما قررت في شرح مختصر الأصول أن أثر التعلق في منع السبب لا في منع الحكم عندنا وإنما امتنع الحكم ضرورة امتناع السبب خلافاً للشافعي فيصير التصرف تطليقاً عند الشرط عندنا وعنده صار تطليقاً زمن التكلم إلى آخره. وأورد عليه ما إذا قال لأجنبية أنت طالق مع نكاحك حيث يتأتى فيه التقرير المذكور أنه لا إذا تزوجها. وحاصل ما أجابوا به أنه يملك التعليق بصريح الشرط وبمعناه بعد النكاح، وأما قبله فلا يملكه إلا بالصريح كـ (إن) ونحوه الموضوعة للتعليق، ولذا صح التعليق بقوله أنت طالق في دخولك الدار ولم يصح قوله لأجنبية أنت طالق في نكاحك. وتعقبه في فتح القدير تبعاً لما في معراج الدراية بأن الدليل إنما قام على ملك اليمين المضافة إلى الملك فتعلق معناه كيفما كان اللفظ والتقييد بلفظ خاص بعد تحقق المعنى تحكم. ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأن الطلاق مع النكاح يتنافيان فلم تصح الحقيقة فيه بخلاف ما نحن فيه لأن الطلاق والعتق لا يتنافيان.
His statement: 'You are divorced twice with the manumission of your master, so he has the right to take her back.' Because he attached the pronouncement of divorce, since it is the cause in reality, to manumission (i'taq) or emancipation ('itq). If the speaker mentioned manumission (i'taq), then no issue. If he mentioned emancipation ('itq), then the intended meaning is manumission because emancipation is its ruling; the ruling is borrowed for the cause, so it is a metaphor. Accordingly, its operation on the word 'iyyaki is either by considering that the action is intended, operating the borrowed word for the infinitive, or by considering that it is operating as the noun of the infinitive, like 'I liked your speech, Zayd'. Otherwise, emancipation is transitive only with an object. We only say it is attached to it despite the fact that the literal meaning of 'with' is accompaniment, because it may be used for the later event, giving it the status of the concomitant by establishing its occurrence afterwards and removing doubt, as in the verse 'For indeed, with hardship [will be] ease' (94:5). So this meaning becomes probable for it, and it is adopted due to the requirement of the presence of the meaning of conditionality for it, which is the dependence of a ruling on the existence of a meaning after it that is non-existent at the time of speaking and is at risk of existence. If manumission is the condition for the pronouncement of divorce, then the pronouncement of two divorces occurs after it, accompanying the emancipation that is later than the manumission, so the divorce occurs after the pronouncement, finding her free, so the husband has the right to take her back. If emancipation is meant, it is more apparent because it accompanies the pronouncement and divorce follows them, so it occurs while she is free. In al-Kafi: because he made the pronouncement connected to emancipation, and that is conceivable only if one is attached to the other as condition to conditioned, or as cause to effect, or both are attached to a single condition or cause and occur at that time. The third is negated because they are not attached to a single condition or cause; likewise the second because the master's manumission is not a cause for the husband's pronouncement, nor is his pronouncement a cause for manumission. So the first aspect is determined. It is impossible for emancipation to be attached to pronouncement because that would remove the owner's ownership without his consent, so the attachment of divorce to manumission is determined. The attached is the pronouncement, not the divorce, according to us, as established in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Usul, because the effect of attachment is to prevent the cause, not to prevent the ruling according to us; the ruling is only prevented due to the prevention of the cause, contrary to al-Shafi'i. So the act becomes a pronouncement at the condition according to us, whereas according to him it becomes a pronouncement at the time of speaking. An objection was raised: the case where one says to a foreign woman 'You are divorced with your marriage,' where the mentioned reasoning would apply, but he does not divorce her when she marries. The gist of their answer is that he has the power of attachment by explicit condition and by its meaning after marriage, but before marriage he only has it by explicit wording like 'if' and similar words used for attachment. Hence, attachment by saying 'You are divorced upon your entering the house' is valid, but saying to a foreign woman 'You are divorced upon your marriage' is not valid. This was critiqued in Fath al-Qadeer following al-Mi'raj al-Diraya: the proof only established ownership of the oath added to ownership, so its meaning is attached however the wording is, and restricting it to a specific wording after the meaning is established is arbitrary. It can be answered that divorce and marriage are mutually exclusive, so the literal meaning is not valid there, unlike our case because divorce and emancipation are not mutually exclusive.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The ruling concerns a husband who says to his wife: 'You are divorced twice with the manumission of your master.' If the master then manumits her, the husband has the right to take her back (raj'ah). The commentary explains the legal reasoning behind this. It first clarifies the wording: if the husband says 'i'taq' (manumission) it is clear; if he says 'itq' (emancipation), it is a metaphor for the act of manumission. The word 'with' (ma'a) is discussed; although its literal meaning is accompaniment, it can imply conditionality, as in the Quranic verse 'with hardship is ease'. Here, the divorce is attached to the manumission as a condition. The text then discusses how the attachment works: the pronouncement of divorce is conditional upon manumission. At the moment of manumission, the wife becomes free, and the divorce occurs while she is free, so the husband retains the right to take her back (since a free woman divorced twice is not irrevocably separated). The commentary also distinguishes between the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the effect of attachment: according to the Hanafis, the attachment prevents the cause, not the ruling itself, so the divorce occurs only when the condition is met. The Shafi'is hold that the divorce occurs at the time of the utterance. An objection is raised: if someone says to a foreign woman 'You are divorced with your marriage', the same reasoning would imply divorce upon marriage, yet it does not occur. The answer is that before marriage, the husband does not have the power to attach divorce to marriage except by explicit conditional wording like 'if'; after marriage he can attach by meaning as well. This is because divorce and marriage are incompatible, whereas divorce and emancipation are not. The commentary then cites a critique from Fath al-Qadeer, but the overall ruling remains: in the given case, the husband has the right to take her back.

Key Terms
مع (ma'a): In this context, it is used to imply conditionality, not mere accompaniment, based on the Quranic usage.
تعليق (ta'liq): The attachment of a legal effect (here divorce) to a future condition, such that the effect occurs only when the condition is met.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his slave wife: 'You are divorced twice with the manumission of your master.' The master then frees her. According to the Hanafi school, what is the husband's right?
A) He has no right to take her back because she was a slave when the divorce was pronounced.
B) He has the right to take her back because she was free at the time the divorce took effect.
C) The divorce is invalid because it was conditional.
D) He must pay compensation to the master.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, conditional divorce takes effect only when the condition is met, so the wife's status at that moment determines the husband's rights.

Ambiguous Divorce with Free and Slave Wives
Rulings on ambiguous divorce between a free and a slave wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine who has the right to clarify an ambiguous divorce or manumission statement (khiyar al-bayan) in scenarios involving free and slave wives, and derive the legal consequences for each scenario.

Core Idea
The right to clarify an ambiguous marital statement belongs to the one who originated the ambiguity, unless they become permanently incapable.

This lesson from al-Muhit al-Burhani presents several scenarios where a man makes an ambiguous divorce or manumission statement involving two wives with different statuses (free/slave). The key is identifying who has the right to specify (khiyar al-bayan) and how that affects the validity of divorce and the possibility of return (raj'ah). By working through each scenario, you will internalize the Hanafi rules for resolving ambiguity in legally consequential utterances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous statement
In each scenario, determine exactly what is unspecified. For example, the husband says 'One of you is divorced twice' without naming which wife.
The ambiguity (ibham) can be in divorce or manumission.
2
Determine the originator of ambiguity
Decide who made the first ambiguous statement. The one who initiated the ambiguity (muwqi') is the one who said 'one of you' or 'the freed one' without specification.
The originator may be the husband or the master.
3
Apply the rule of khiyar al-bayan
According to the Hanafi school, the right to clarify belongs to the originator of the ambiguity. If that person becomes permanently incapable (e.g., dies), the right passes to the other party.
This rule ensures that the party who created the uncertainty resolves it.
4
Derive legal consequences
After clarification, determine the wife's status (free or slave) at the moment the divorce took effect. This affects the number of divorces required for irrevocability (ba'in) and the right of raj'ah.
A free woman becomes ba'in after three divorces; a slave after two. Raj'ah is only possible if the divorce is revocable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : رجل تحته حرة وأمة دخل بهما فقال إحداكما طالق ثنتين فأعتقت الأمة فعين الطلاق في الأمة في مرضه طلقت ثنتين ولا تحل إلا بزوج الطلاق المبهم في حق الموقع نازل رجل تحته أمتان فقال المولى إحداكما حرة فقال الزوج المعتقة طالق ثنتين فالخيار للمولى لأن الزوج جعل إيقاعه بناء على إيقاع المولى العتق، وخيار البيان لمن هو الأصل في الإبهام وهو المولى، وملك الزوج الرجعة لأنه طلق في حال الحرية والحرية لا تحرم بالثنتين ولو قال الزوج إحداكما طالق ثنتين فقال المولى المطلقة معتقة فالبيان إلى الزوج لأنه هو المجمل ولا يملك الزوج الرجعة لأن الطلاق صادفها وهي أمة فتحرم بالثنتين، فإن مات المولى في الصورة الأولى قبل البيان عتق نصف كل واحدة وخير الزوج في بيان المطلقة لوقوع اليأس بموت المولى فجعل البيان إلى الزوج بخلاف ما لو غاب المولى لا يجبر الزوج على البيان لعدم اليأس ا هـ .
And in al-Muhit: A man who has a free woman and a slave-girl under his marriage, and he had intercourse with both, then said: 'One of you is divorced twice.' The slave-girl was then freed, and he specified the divorce for the slave-girl in his illness: she is divorced twice and is not lawful except after marriage to another husband (the divorce is ambiguous regarding the one who initiated it). A man has two slave-girls under him; the master said: 'One of you is free.' Then the husband said: 'The freed one is divorced twice.' In this case, the option (to specify) belongs to the master, because the husband made his pronouncement based on the master's pronouncement of manumission, and the right of clarification belongs to the one who originated the ambiguity, which is the master. The husband has the right to take her back because he divorced her while she was free, and freedom is not made unlawful by two divorces. If the husband said: 'One of you is divorced twice,' and the master then said: 'The divorced one is free,' then the clarification belongs to the husband because he is the one who made the ambiguous statement, and the husband does not have the right to take her back because the divorce found her as a slave, so she becomes unlawful by two divorces. If the master dies in the first scenario before clarification, half of each slave is freed, and the husband is given the choice to specify the divorced one, because despair has occurred with the death of the master, so the clarification is given to the husband. In contrast, if the master is absent, the husband is not compelled to clarify due to lack of despair.
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage from al-Muhit al-Burhani presents multiple scenarios involving ambiguous divorce between two wives, one free and one slave. The rulings hinge on who has the right to clarify the ambiguity (khiyar al-bayan) and the status of the wife at the time divorce takes effect. In the first scenario, if the husband ambiguously divorces one of two wives (free and slave), and later the slave is freed, and then in his illness he specifies the slave, she becomes divorced twice and is not halal for him until she marries another man (because a slave divorced twice becomes irrevocable, but since she was freed before the divorce, the ruling follows her status at divorce? Actually the text says 'fateen al-talaq fi al-ama' meaning he specified the slave-girl as the one divorced. She is divorced twice and does not become halal except after marrying another husband (i.e., the divorce is binding). The phrase 'al-talaq al-mubham fi haqq al-muwqi' nazil' means the ambiguous divorce is considered as if it were from the one who initiated it (the husband). In the second scenario, a man has two slave wives; the master says 'one of you is free', then the husband says 'the freed one is divorced twice'. Here the right to clarify belongs to the master, because he originated the ambiguity (by freeing one of two). The husband then divorces the freed one based on his statement; the husband has the right of raj'ah because she was free at divorce. In the third scenario, the husband first says 'one of you is divorced twice', then the master says 'the divorced one is free'. Here the husband is the one who made the ambiguous statement, so he has the right to clarify, but he does not have raj'ah because the divorce took effect while she was still a slave (since the master's statement came later). Finally, if the master dies before clarifying the first scenario, half of each slave is freed, and the husband gets the right to specify the divorced one because the master can no longer clarify. If the master is merely absent, the husband is not forced to clarify.

Key Terms
خيار البيان (khiyar al-bayan): The right to clarify an ambiguous statement (here, which wife is meant by the divorce or manumission).
إبهام (ibham): Ambiguity in a statement where the subject is not specified, requiring clarification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a husband has two slave wives, the master says 'One of you is free,' and the husband says 'The freed one is divorced twice.' The master later specifies which slave is freed. What is the husband's right of raj'ah?
A) He has no right of raj'ah because he divorced a slave.
B) He has the right of raj'ah because the wife was free at the time of divorce.
C) He has the right of raj'ah only if the master specifies within the iddah.
D) He has no right of raj'ah because the divorce was based on an ambiguous statement.

Key Takeaway
When ambiguous divorce or manumission involves multiple wives of different statuses, the right to clarify belongs to the originator of the ambiguity, and the legal consequences depend on the wife's status at the moment the divorce takes effect after clarification.

Cause and Effect in Talaq: The Simultaneous Condition Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on raj'ah after simultaneous conditional divorce and manumission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of simultaneous conditional divorce and manumission on raj'ah and identify the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
When cause and effect are simultaneous, a conditional divorce takes effect before manumission, preventing raj'ah.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about a scenario where both a master's manumission and a husband's divorce are conditioned on the same future event. The ruling hinges on the philosophical question of whether a legal cause (illa) and its effect (ma'lul) occur simultaneously or sequentially. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply usul al-fiqh reasoning to fiqh cases.

Guided Path
1
State the Scenario
Describe the case: a master says to his slave-girl 'If tomorrow comes, you are free', and her husband says 'If tomorrow comes, you are divorced twice'. When tomorrow comes, what happens?
The same condition triggers both manumission and divorce.
2
Identify the Positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the husband has no right of raj'ah. Muhammad holds that he does. Recognize which position is in the zahir al-riwayah.
The dispute is within the Hanafi school itself.
3
Understand the Underlying Principle
The disagreement stems from the nature of cause (illa) and effect (ma'lul). The majority view (including Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) holds that cause and effect are simultaneous. Muhammad holds that the effect follows the cause immediately but sequentially.
This is a usul al-fiqh and philosophical debate.
4
Apply to the Scenario
If cause and effect are simultaneous, the divorce takes effect at the same instant as manumission, but the wife is still considered a slave at that instant, so two divorces are irrevocable (no raj'ah). If effect follows cause, manumission occurs first, making her free before divorce; thus one divorce is revocable.
The sequence determines whether she is a slave or free at the moment of divorce.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: If a judge follows the two, the husband cannot take her back. If he follows Muhammad, he can. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the two.
This shows how a theoretical disagreement affects actual rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو تعلق عتقها وطلقتاها بمجيء الغد فجاء لا يعني لو قال المولى لأمته إذا جاء غد فأنت حرة وقال زوجها إذا جاء غد فأنت طالق ثنتين فجاء الغد لا يملك الزوج الرجعة عندهما خلافاً لمحمد، والأصل فيه أن العلة والمعلول يقترنان عند الجمهور في الخارج، ومنهم من قال إن المعلول يعقبها بلا فصل ومنهم خصوا العلل الشرعية فجعلوها تستعقب المعلول بخلاف العقلية كالاستطاعة الفعل، واختار القول الثاني في فتح القدير سواء كانت عقلية أو شرعية حتى إن الانكسار يعقب الكسر في الخارج غير أنه لسرعة إعقابه مع قلة الزمن إلى الغاية إذا كان آنياً لم يقع تمييز التقدم والتأخر فيهما، وهذا لأن المؤثر لا يقوم به التأثير قبل وجوده وحالة خروجه من العدم لم يكن ثابتاً فلا بد من أن تكمل هويته ليقوم به عارض وإلا لم يكن مؤثراً. وفي التلويح : لا نزاع في تقدم العلة على المعلول بمعنى
His statement: 'And if her manumission and her two divorces were attached to the coming of tomorrow, then it does not mean...' i.e., if the master says to his slave-girl 'If tomorrow comes, you are free' and her husband says 'If tomorrow comes, you are divorced twice', then when tomorrow comes, the husband does not have the right to take her back according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), contrary to Muhammad. The basis of this is that cause and effect are simultaneous according to the majority; some say that the effect follows without interval; some specifically for legal causes make them set the effect after them, unlike physical causes like capability. The second opinion is chosen in Fath al-Qadeer, whether legal or physical, such that even breakage follows the fracture in the external world, except that due to the speed of succession with minimal time, if it is instantaneous, the precedence and posteriority are not distinguished. This is because the effect does not arise from the cause before its existence, and at the moment of emerging from non-existence it was not established, so its identity must be completed for a property to arise, otherwise it would not be effective. In al-Talwih: There is no dispute about the precedence of the cause over the effect in the sense...
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage discusses a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding a scenario where both a master and a husband make conditional statements dependent on the same future event: the master says to his slave-girl 'If tomorrow comes, you are free,' and the husband says 'If tomorrow comes, you are divorced twice.' When tomorrow comes, the issue is whether the husband has the right to take her back (raj'ah). According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf ('the two'), the husband does not have raj'ah, while Muhammad says he does. The reason for this difference lies in their understanding of the relationship between cause ('illa) and effect (ma'lul). The majority of theologians hold that cause and effect occur simultaneously. However, some say the effect follows the cause without delay. Others distinguish between legal causes (which set the effect after them) and physical causes (which are simultaneous with their effects). Fath al-Qadeer chooses the second opinion (effect follows cause) for both types. The commentary then provides a philosophical explanation: the cause must be fully actualized for the effect to arise; if the cause is instantaneous, the effect follows immediately but the sequence may be imperceptible. This dispute affects the timing of the divorce relative to the manumission. If divorce occurs after manumission, the wife is free when divorced, so the husband retains raj'ah. If divorce occurs simultaneously or before, she is still a slave, and two divorces make her forbidden. The passage from al-Talwih confirms that there is no dispute that the cause precedes the effect in some sense, but the precise timing is debated.

Key Terms
علة (illa): In this context, a cause that brings about a legal ruling (hukm); the master's statement is the cause of manumission.
معلول (ma'lul): The effect or result of a cause; here, the manumission or divorce is the effect of the conditional statement.
رجعة (raj'ah): The right of a husband to take back his wife during her waiting period (iddah) after a revocable divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, in the scenario where both manumission and two divorces are conditional on the same future event, what is the husband's right regarding raj'ah?
A) He has raj'ah because the wife becomes free first.
B) He does not have raj'ah because the wife is still a slave when divorced.
C) He must seek judicial permission.
D) The divorce is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on simultaneity of cause and effect at the moment a condition is fulfilled determines whether raj'ah is available after a conditional divorce and manumission.

Indicating Divorce Number by Finger Gesture: Spread vs Closed
Sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq on finger gesture in talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the number of divorces when the husband says 'أنت طالق هكذا' and points with fingers, and explain the distinction between enforcement in court (qaḍāʾ) and acceptance in conscience (diyānah).

Core Idea
Spread fingers determine divorce count in court; closed fingers require intention, accepted only in conscience.

In Hanafi law, the number of divorces can be indicated by a gesture accompanying the utterance. But not every gesture is equally binding. This lesson unpacks a key ruling: when a husband says 'أنت طالق هكذا' and points with his fingers, the number of divorces depends on whether the fingers are spread (منشورة) or closed (مضمومة), and whether the matter is judged in court or in conscience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance and gesture
The husband says 'أنت طالق هكذا' and simultaneously points with his fingers. The word 'هكذا' links the gesture to the divorce count via comparison (تشبيه).
Without 'هكذا', the gesture is ignored; the divorce defaults to one.
2
Determine if fingers are spread or closed
If the fingers are spread (منشورة), the number of fingers indicates the intended number of divorces. If closed (مضمومة), the gesture is ambiguous.
The default in custom and sunnah is spread fingers.
3
Apply the court rule (qaḍāʾ)
In court, only the apparent gesture matters. Spread fingers → number of divorces = number of fingers. Closed fingers → only one divorce is enforced, as the indication is unclear.
Intention cannot override the apparent meaning in court.
4
Consider intention in conscience (diyānah)
If the husband intended a different number (e.g., closed fingers meaning one, or palm meaning one), he may be truthful in conscience before Allah, but the court does not accept this.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the Hanafi approach to evidentiary rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب الطلاق ثلاث حيض أنت طالق هكذا أشار بثلاثة أصابع فهي ثلاث أنت طالق بائن أو البتة أو احتياجه إليها ويسمى التقديم بالعلية وبالذات ولا في مقارنة العلة العقلية لمعلولها بالزمان كيلا يلزم التخلف والخلاف في العلل الشرعية ا هـ. وإذا عرف هذا فمن الأوجه لمحمد أنهما لما تعلقا بشرط واحد وجب أن تطلق زمن نزول الحرية فيصادفها وهي حرة لاقترانهما وجوداً فلا تحرم بها حرمة غليظة . قلنا : المتعلقان بشرط واحد يقتضي أن يصادفها على الحالة التي صادفها عليها العتق وهي الرق فتغلظ الحرمة بلا شك بخلاف المسألة الأولى لأن الإعتاق هناك شرط فيقع الطلاق بعده قوله : (وعدتها ثلاث حيض) يعني في المسألتين اتفاقاً كما في المحيط لأنها حكم الطلاق فتعقبه أو لأنه يحتاط فيها وكذا يحتاط في الحرمة الغليظة. ولو كان الزوج مريضاً لا ترث منه لأنه حين تكلم بالطلاق لم يقصد الفرار إذ لم يكن لها حق في ماله، ولأن العتق والطلاق يقعان معاً ثم الطلاق يصادفها وهي رقيقة فلا ميراث لها. كذا في المبسوط . قوله : أنت طالق هكذا وأشار بثلاثة أصابع فهي ثلاث لأن هذا تشبيه بعدد المشار إليه العدد المفاد كميته بالأصابع المشار إليه بذا لأن الهاء للتنبيه والكاف للتشبيه وذا للإشارة . قيد بقوله «بثلاث» لأنه لو أشار بواحدة فواحدة أو ثنتين فثنتان وأشار بقوله «وأشار إلى أن الإشارة تقع بالمنشورة منها دون المضمومة للعرف وللسنة ولو نوى الإشارة بالمضمومتين صدق ديانة لاقضاء، وكذا لو نوى الإشارة بالكف والإشارة بالكف أن تقع الأصابع كلها منشورة . قوله : (قيد بقوله بثلاث (الخ قال الرملي : وقيد بقوله أنت طالق لأنه لو قال أنت هكذا فهو لغو ولو نوى الطلاق لأن اللفظ لا يشعر به والنية لا تؤثر بغير لفظ . قال الزيلعي في تعليل أصل المسألة : لأن الإشارة بالأصابع تفيد العلم بالعدد عرفاً وشرعاً إذا اقترنت بالاسم المهم ا هـ طلاق هنا يشار إليه به فتأمل. ولم أر من صرح به في هذا المحل إلى الآن ثم راجعت أحكام الإشارة من الأشباه والنظائر فوجدته قال : ولم أر الآن أنت هكذا مشيراً بأصابعه ولم يقل طالق ا هـ . أقول : وقد رأيت الحكم كما ذكرته بالعلة المذكورة في كتب الشافعية كشرح الروض لشيخ الإسلام زكريا وغيره ولا شيء من قواعدنا ينافيه فتأمل قوله : (والإشارة بالكف الخ) قال في الفتح والإشارة تقع بالمنشورة، ولو نوى الإشارة بالمضمومتين يصدق ديانة لا قضاء وكذا إذا نوى الإشارة بالكف. في الدراية : الإشارة بالكف أن تقع الأصابع كلها منشورة فالذي يثبت بالنية منه أن تكون الأصابع الثلاث منشورة فقط حتى يقع في الأولى ثنتان ديانة وفي الثانية واحدة لأنه يحتمله لكنه خلاف الظاهر ا هـ . قلت : وحاصل كلام الفتح المذكور أنه إذا كانت الثلاث منشورة تقع ثلاث وتصح فيها نيته ديانة في الأولى أي فيما إذا نوى الإشارة بالمضمومتين فتقع ثنتان وكذا تصح نيته ديانة في الثانية أي فيما إذا نوى الإشارة بالكف فتقع واحدة ولما كان خلاف الظاهر من كون المراد المنشورة دون المضمومة ودون الكف لم يصدق قضاء ومقتضى هذا الكلام أنه إذا كانت الأصابع كلها منشورة.

أفحش الطلاق أو طلاق الشيطان أو البدعة أو كالجبل أو أشد الطلاق أو كالف أو ملء منشورة، وهذا هو المعتمد، وهناك أقوال ذكرها في المعراج : الأول عن بعض المتأخرين لو جعل ظهر الكف إليها والأصابع المنشورة إلى نفسه دين قضاء ولو جعل ظهر الكف إلى نفسه وبطون الأصابع إليها لا يصدق في القضاء الثاني لو كان باطن الكف إلى السماء فالعبرة إلى النشر، وإن كان إلى الأرض فالعبرة إلى الضم والثالث إن كان نشراً عن ضم فالعبرة للنشر، وإن كان ضماً عن نشر فالعبرة للضم اعتباراً للعادة ا هـ. وقيد بقوله «هكذا» لأنه لو قال أنت طالق وأشار بأصابعه ولم يقل «هكذا فهي واحدة لفقد التشبيه المتقدم، وفي المحيط : وكذا لو قالت لزوجها طلقني فأشار إليها بثلاثة أصابع وأراد به ثلاث تطليقات لا يقع ما لم يقل «هكذا لأنه لو وقع وقع بالضمير والطلاق لا يقع بالضمير ا هـ. لو قال أنت طالق مثل هذا وأشار بأصابعه الثلاث يقع ثلاث إن نوى ثلاثاً وإلا فواحدة، هكذا في المبتغى بالمعجمة، فقد فرقوا هنا الكاف و «مثل» بناء على أن الكاف للتشبيه في الذات و «مثلاً» بین للتشبيه في الصفات، ولذا نقل عن الإمام الأعظم رضي الله عنه أنه قال : أيماني كأيمان جبريل عليه السلام ولا أقول أيماني مثل أيمان جبريل صلوات الله عليه وسلامه. وفي ونوى الكف أنه يصدق قضاء وديانة لأنه خص صحة نية الكف ديانة بما إذا كانت الثلاث منشورة، وهذا خلاف ما فهمه المؤلف فإن المتبادر من كلامه أنه يصدق ديانة في نية الإشارة بالكف إذا كانت الأصابع كلها منشورة. وبما ذكرناه يحصل التوفيق بين ما هنا وما ذكره القهستاني من أنه لو نوى الإشارة بالكف صدق قضاء بخلاف ما إذا نوى المعقودتين ا هـ . . فيحمل كلام القهستاني على ما إذا كانت كلها منشورة، وكلام غيره من أنه يصدق ديانة فقط على ما إذا كان بعضها منشوراً ووجهه ظاهر، فإن نشر الكل قرينة على أنه لم يرد الإشارة بالأصابع بل أراد الكف، ويظهر أن مثله ما لو كانت كل الأصابع مضمومة بخلاف ما إذا كان بعضها منشوراً فإن الظاهر أنه أراد الإشارة بالمنشورة فلا يصدق قضاء أنه أراد المضموم منها أو الكف ويصدق ديانة فقط لأنه محتمل كلامه. هذا ما ظهر لي هنا فتأمله قوله : (وهذا هو المعتمد أي ما ذكره من اعتبار المنشورة دون المضمومة بلا تفصيل هو المعتمد، ويدل عليه حكاية الأقوال ،بعده، وكذا قول الفتح بعد حكاية الأقوال المذكورة والمعول عليه إطلاق المصنف اهـ. . فليس قوله «وهذا هو المعتمد راجعاً إلى قوله والإشارة الخ» كما فهمه العلائي قوله : (ولم يقل هكذا فهي واحدة قال الرملي وإن نوى به الثلاث كما في التتارخانية عن الخانية وبه يعلم جواب ما يقع من الأتراك من رمى ثلاث حصوات قائلاً أنت هكذا ولا ينطق بلفظ الطلاق وهو عدم الوقوع تأمل ا هـ . وفي علمه هذا تأمل بل هو مثل قوله أنت هكذا مشيراً بأصابعه من فحقه أن يذكر في القولة السابقة تأمل قوله : (الفقد التشبيه لأنه كما لا يتحقق الطلاق بدون اللفظ لا يتحقق عدده بدونه كذا في القهستاني قوله : (لأنه لو وقع بالضمير الظاهر أن المراد به الضمير القلبي لا النحوي قوله : ( وإلا فواحدة قال في النهر : أي بائنة كقوله أنت طالق كألف». كذا في
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Divorce: Three menstrual cycles; 'You are divorced like this' and he pointed with three fingers, then it is three. 'You are divorced absolutely' or 'finally' or... The waiting period is three menstrual cycles by agreement... If the husband was sick, she does not inherit from him... The author's statement: 'You are divorced like this and he pointed with three fingers, then it is three' because this is a comparison by number... He restricted it with 'with three' because if he pointed with one then it is one, or two then two. And he said 'and he pointed' to indicate that the indication is by spread fingers, not closed, according to custom and sunnah. If he intended the closed fingers, he is believed in conscience but not in court... And the intention of the palm... The relied-upon position is as mentioned.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the ruling when a husband indicates the number of divorces by pointing with his fingers while saying 'أنت طالق هكذا'. The author establishes that if the husband says 'أنت طالق هكذا' and points with three spread fingers, three divorces occur. The key condition is that the fingers must be spread (منشورة) according to custom and sunnah; if they are closed (مضمومة), the indication is not considered in court unless the husband intended a different number, and then it is only accepted in conscience (ديانة). Similarly, if he points with the entire palm (كف) and all fingers are spread, it counts as one, but if he intended the palm, it is accepted in conscience. The passage also discusses the waiting period (عدتها ثلاث حيض) and the case of a sick husband who does not inherit. The relied-upon (mu'tamad) position is the spread fingers without detail.

Key Terms
منشورة (manshūrah): Spread fingers, i.e., the fingers are extended apart, indicating the number clearly.
مضمومة (maḍmūmah): Closed fingers, i.e., the fingers are together, which is ambiguous in indicating number.
كف (kaff): Palm of the hand; pointing with the entire palm when all fingers are spread indicates one of something.
ديانة (diyānah): In conscience; refers to what is accepted between the individual and Allah, not enforceable in court.
قضاء (qaḍāʾ): In court; refers to what is enforceable by a judge based on apparent evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says 'أنت طالق هكذا' and points with three spread fingers, but later claims he intended only one divorce, what is the ruling in court?
A) One divorce, because his intention is accepted.
B) Three divorces, because the apparent gesture is clear.
C) The divorce is invalid because the gesture is not proper.
D) It depends on the judge's discretion.

Key Takeaway
When a husband says 'أنت طالق هكذا' and points with spread fingers, the number of divorces equals the number of fingers; closed fingers or palm indicate only one in court, and intention is accepted only in conscience.

Talaq by Similitude with Weights (Sanjah)
Ruling on talaq by similitude with weights (sanjah)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the number of divorces when a husband uses similitude with weights by applying the Hanafi rule that intention governs tashbih, and if no intention, one ba'in divorce falls.

Core Idea
In tashbih with weights, intention decides number; without intention, one ba'in falls.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with a specific scenario in divorce: when a husband says 'you are divorced like a weight of X'. The Hanafi jurists analyze whether such a statement implies one divorce or multiple based on the weights mentioned. The key principle is that similitude (tashbih) can be either in description (strength) or in number, and the husband's intention determines which applies. Without explicit intention, the default is description, yielding one ba'in divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the husband doing? He is pronouncing divorce with a similitude involving weights. The ruling: if no intention, one ba'in divorce falls.
Ba'in means irrevocable divorce.
2
Locate the dalil – the analogy (qiyas)
The author cites al-Muhit, which first establishes the ruling for 'like a thousand' (ka-alf) as one ba'in. Then by analogy (qiyas), the same applies to any weight similitude unless the number of weights indicates a different number.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah – the effective cause (illa)
The illa is that the similitude is taken as description (strength) rather than number, unless the context (number of weights) makes number evident. The default is the lesser (one divorce) because it is the minimum.
4
Apply the qiyas to the examples
The author works through examples: one weight → one divorce; two weights → two divorces; three weights → three divorces. If the husband explicitly intends a number, that number applies.
5
Note the authority and cross-madhhab status
This ruling is from al-Muhit (a later Hanafi source) and is mukhtalaf fih – other schools may hold differently. The Hanafi mufta bihi is one ba'in without intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البيت أو تطليقه شديدة أو طويلة أو عريضة فهي واحدة بائنه إن لم ينو ثلاثاً .
البدائع : أنه يحتمل التشبيه من حيث العدد ويحتمل التشبيه في الصفة وهو الشبه فأيهما نوى صحت نيته وإن لم تكن له نية يحمل على التشبيه من حيث الصفة لأنه أدنى ا هـ . وفي المحيط : إذا لم ينو الثلاث تقع واحدة بائنة كما في قوله أنت طالق كألف، وعلى قياس هذا لو قال أنت طالق مثل سنجة دائق تقع واحدة لأن له سنجة واحدة فقد شبه الواقع بالواحدة، ولو قال مثل سنجة دائق ونصف أو دانقين تقع ثنتان لأن له سنجتين فقد شبه الواقع بالعددين، ولو قال مثل سنجة دانقين ونصف تقع الثلاث لأنه يوزن بثلاث سنجات، ولو قال مثل سنجة نصف درهم تقع واحدة، ولو قال مثل سنجة ثلثي درهم فتقع ثنتان لأن له سنجتين، ولو قال سنجة ثلاثة أرباع درهم تقع ثلاث لأنه له ثلاث سنجات، ولو قال مثل سنجة ألف درهم تقع واحدة ا هـ .
Or 'the house' or 'his divorce is severe' or 'long' or 'wide', it is one ba'in divorce unless he intended three. Al-Bada'i': It can carry similitude in terms of number or similitude in description; whichever he intended, his intention is valid. If he had no intention, it is taken to be similitude in description because that is the lesser. End. In al-Muhit: If he did not intend three, one ba'in divorce occurs, as when he says 'you are divorced like a thousand'. By analogy with this, if he says 'you are divorced like a weight of one daniq', one occurs because he has one weight, so he likened the actual to one. If he says 'like a weight of one and a half daniq' or 'two daniq', two occur because he has two weights, so he likened to two. If he says 'like a weight of two and a half daniq', three occur because it is weighed with three weights. If he says 'like a weight of half a dirham', one occurs. If he says 'like a weight of two-thirds of a dirham', two occur because he has two weights. If he says 'like a weight of three-quarters of a dirham', three occur because he has three weights. If he says 'like a weight of a thousand dirhams', one occurs.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on divorce by similitude (tashbih). The author quotes the matn stating that phrases like 'or the house' or 'his divorce is severe' etc. result in one ba'in divorce unless three are intended. Then he cites al-Bada'i' to explain that similitude can be in number or in description. If the husband intends a specific number, his intention is valid; otherwise, it is taken as description. Next, from al-Muhit, he provides detailed analogical examples: if the husband says 'like a weight of one daniq', since there is only one weight, it implies one divorce. If he says 'like a weight of one and a half daniq', there are two weights (one for the whole and one for the half), so two divorces. For 'two and a half daniq', three weights are needed, so three. This analogy (qiyas) follows the principle that the number of weights indicates the intended number of divorces. The key point is that without explicit intention, the safer assumption is the minimum, which is one ba'in divorce.

Key Terms
سنجة (sanjah): A weight used in weighing; here it refers to the specific weight pieces used to measure a certain amount (e.g., one daniq, half dirham).
التشبيه (al-tashbih): Similitude: comparing the divorce to something else, potentially in strength or number.
دائق (dā'iq): A small weight unit; approximately 0.5 grams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Two ba'in divorces
C) Three ba'in divorces
D) One raj'i divorce

Key Takeaway
In talaq by similitude with weights, the number of divorces depends on the number of distinct weight pieces mentioned, but without intention, the default is one ba'in divorce unless the weights clearly indicate a specific number.
