Descriptive Divorce Phrases: One Ba'in Unless Three Intended
Ruling that descriptive phrases yield one ba'in divorce unless three intended

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify descriptive divorce phrases and determine whether they result in one ba'in or three, applying the Hanafi principle of wasf vs. 'adad.

Core Idea
Descriptive phrases indicate quality, not quantity—one ba'in unless the phrase explicitly denotes number.

When a husband uses descriptive expressions like 'ba'in' or 'al-battah' without specifying a number, the Hanafi school rules that only one ba'in divorce occurs. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling, analyzing each phrase and the conditions that might change the outcome, such as intention or the presence of a numerical indicator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the descriptive phrase
Read the husband's utterance and extract the descriptive term (e.g., 'ba'in', 'al-battah', 'afhash al-talaq', etc.). Note whether it is a simple adjective, a superlative, a simile, or a number-related expression.
Each category carries a default ruling.
2
Classify as wasf (quality) or 'adad (number)
If the phrase is a pure description (e.g., 'ba'in' meaning irrevocable), it falls under wasf and indicates one ba'in unless three is intended. If the phrase explicitly indicates a number (e.g., 'akthar' meaning most numerous), it falls under 'adad and results in three without intention.
The exception is 'akthar' because it implies multiplicity.
3
Apply the default ruling and check for intention
For wasf phrases, the default is one ba'in. If the husband intended three, then three ba'in occur. If he intended two in a compound expression, see the rules in the text about separate statements.
Intention matters only if the word is actually uttered; mere thought does not change the ruling.
4
Handle similitude phrases (tashbih)
Phrases like 'ka-al-jabal' or 'ka-alf' involve comparison. For 'ka-alf', the similitude can be in strength (one ba'in) or in number (three if intended). If no intention, the lesser (one ba'in) is presumed.
Explicit number comparisons like 'like the number of a thousand' yield three without intention.
5
Resolve apparent contradictions
Some phrases like 'talaq al-shaytan' or 'talaq al-bid'a' may have special timing conditions (fall immediately or after a condition). The text confirms the overall ruling of one ba'in regardless, but actual occurrence timing differs as per earlier fiqh rules.
The ruling of one ba'in is consistent; the timing does not contradict it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (أنت طالق بائن أو البتة أو أفحش الطلاق أو طلاق الشيطان أو البدعة أو كالجبل أو أشد الطلاق أو كالف أو ملء البيت أو تطليقة شديدة أو طويلة أو عريضة فهي واحدة بائنة إن لم ينو (ثلاثاً) بيان للطلاق البائن بعد بيان الرجعي. وإنما كان بائناً في هذه لأنه وصف الطلاق بما يحتمله وهو البينونة فإنه يثبت به البينونة قبل الدخول للحال، وكذا عند ذكر المال وبعده إذا انقضت العدة وأورد عليه أنه لو احتمل البينونة لصحت إرادتها بطالق وقد قدمنا عدم صحتها. وأجيب بأن عمل النية في الملفوظ لا في غيره، ولفظ بائن لم يصر ملفوظاً به بالنية بخلاف طالق بائن وفيه نظر مذكور في فتح القدير. قيد بكون بائن صفة بلا عطف ثنتين لا يصح لكونه عدداً محضاً إلا إذا عنى بأنت طالق واحدة وبقوله بائن أو البتة أو نحوهما أخرى يقع تطليقتان بناء على أن التركيب خبر بعد خبر وهما بائنتان لأن بينونة الأولى ضرورة بينونة الثانية إذ معنى الرجعي كونه بحيث يملك رجعتها وذلك منتف باتصال البائنة الثانية فلا فائدة في وصفها بالرجعية، وكل كناية قرنت بطالق يجري فيها ذلك فيقع ثنتان بائنتان. وأشار بأفحش الطلاق إلى كل وصف على أفعل لأنه للتفاوت وهو يحصل بالبينونة وهو أفحش من الطلاق الرجعي فدخل أخبث الطلاق وأشره وأخشنه وأكبره وأغلظه وأطوله وأعرضه وأعظمه إلا قوله «أكثره» بالثاء المثلثة فإنه يقع به الثلاث، ولا يدين إذا قال نويت واحدة وإنما وقع البائن بطلاق الشيطان والبدعة لأن الرجعي هو السني غالباً فلا أن یرد الرجعي قد لا يكون سنياً كالطلاق الصريح في الحيض. فإن قلت : قد تقدم في الطلاق البدعي أنه لو قال أنت طالق للبدعة أو طلاق البدعة ولا نية له، فإن كان في طهر فيه جماع أو في حالة الحيض أو النفاس وقعت واحدة من ساعته، وإن كانت في طهر لا جماع فيه لا يقع في الحال حتى تحيض أو يجامعها في ذلك الطهر كما في البدائع وفتح القدير . قلت : لا منافاة بينهما لأن ما ذكروه هنا هو وقوع الواحدة البائنة بلا نية أعم من كونها تقع الساعة أو بعد وجود شيء. وأشار بقوله «كالجبل» إلى التشبيه بما يوجب زيادة في العظم وهو بزيادة وصف البينونة فيدخل فيه مثل الجبل، وأما البينونة بأشد الطلاق فلأنه وصفه بالشدة لأن أفعل يراد به الوصف فلذا لم يكن للثلاث بلا نية لأن أفعل التفضيل بعض ما أضيف إليه فكان أشد معبراً به عن المصدر الذي هو الطلاق. وأما البينونة بقوله «كألف» فلأن التشبيه يحتمل أن يكون في القوة، ويحتمل أن يكون في العدد، فإن نوى الثاني وقع الثلاث، وإن لم ينو ثبت الأقل وهو البينونة، ودخل فيه مثل ألف ومثل ثلاث وواحدة كالألف إلا أنه في هذه إذا نوى الثلاث لا تقع إلا واحدة، اتفاقاً لأن الواحدة لا تحتمل الثلاث. كذا في الجوهرة. وخرج عنه كعدد الألف وكعدد الثلاث فإنه يقع الثلاث بلا نية، ودخل فيه أيضاً ما لو شبه بالعدد فيما لا عدد فيه كعدد الشمس أو التراب أو قال مثله لأن التشبيه يقتضي ضرباً من الزيادة وهو بالبينونة موجود. بتقدير المصدر كما في البتة فإنه في معنى طلاقاً البتة، وكذا في أفحش الطلاق فإنه في معنى طلاقاً أفحش الطلاق، وهكذا في البواقي قوله : (بالثاء المثلثة) وأما ما في متن التنوير من ضبطه بالتاء المثناة من فوق فصوابه المثلثة كما نبه عليه الرملي في حواشي المنح وقال : إن الحكم صحيح في ذلك أيضاً وذكر في فتاواه ونحوه وأفتى بالثلاث فيه أيضاً . قلت : ويمكن أن يجاب بأنه قصد التنبيه على التعبير بالمثلثة بالأولى تأمل قوله : (لا يقع في الحال حتى تحيض أو يجامعها في ذلك الطهر) قال في النهر :
His saying: ('You are divorced irrevocably (ba'in), or absolutely (al-battah), or the most repulsive divorce (afhash al-talaq), or the divorce of Satan (talaq al-shaytan), or of innovation (al-bid'a), or like a mountain (ka-al-jabal), or the most severe divorce (ashadd al-talaq), or like a thousand (ka-alf), or filling the house (mil' al-bayt), or a severe divorce (tatliqa shadida), or long (tawila), or wide (arida), then it is one ba'in divorce if he did not intend three) is an explanation of the ba'in divorce after explaining the raj'i. It is ba'in here because he described the divorce with that which carries it, namely irrevocability (al-baynuna); so it establishes revocability before consummation immediately, and similarly when mentioning wealth and after it if the 'idda expires. An objection was raised: if it carries the possibility of revocation, then it would be valid to intend it by saying just 'taliq'; but we already stated its invalidity. The answer is that the effect of intention is on the uttered word, not on something else; and the word 'ba'in' did not become uttered by intention, unlike 'taliq ba'in'. But there is a point to consider in this, mentioned in Fath al-Qadir. It is conditioned that 'ba'in' be an adjective without conjunction, because if he said 'you are divorced and ba'in' or 'you are divorced then ba'in' and he said 'I intended nothing by my saying ba'in', then it is raj'i; but if he mentioned it with the particle 'fa' (and the rest as it is), it is ba'in. This is in al-Dhakhira. He indicated by his saying 'then it is one if he did not intend three' that if he intended two, it is invalid because it is a pure number; except if he meant by 'you are divorced' one, and by his saying 'ba'in' or 'al-battah' etc. another, then two divorces occur, based on the construction being one statement after another, and both are ba'in because the first's irrevocability necessarily entails the second's irrevocability, since the meaning of raj'i is that he can take her back, which is negated by the connection of a ba'in second; so there is no benefit in describing it as raj'i. And every kinaya (indirect expression) coupled with 'taliq' takes this ruling, so two ba'in divorces fall. He indicated by 'afhash al-talaq' to every description on the pattern 'af'al' because it indicates variance, which is achieved by irrevocability, and it is more repulsive than raj'i divorce; so the most contemptible, most evil, most rough, greatest, most severe, longest, widest, weakest divorce are included, except his saying 'akthar' (most) with the letter tha (ث), for it results in three, and he is not believed if he says he intended one. The ba'in occurs by 'talaq al-shaytan' and 'talaq al-bid'a' because raj'i is usually sunni, although it is possible that raj'i may not be sunni, like explicit divorce during menstruation. If you say: It was previously mentioned in the section on bid'a divorce that if he says 'you are divorced for bid'a' or 'divorce of bid'a' and has no intention, then if it was in a period of purity with intercourse or during menstruation or postpartum, one divorce falls immediately; but if it was in a period of purity without intercourse, it does not fall immediately until she menstruates or he has intercourse with her in that purity, as in al-Bada'i' and Fath al-Qadir. I say: There is no contradiction because what they mentioned here is the occurrence of one ba'in divorce without intention, regardless of whether it falls immediately or after something. He indicated by 'ka-al-jabal' the similitude that necessitates an increase in greatness, and that is by increasing the description of irrevocability, so 'like a mountain' and similar are included. As for 'ashadd al-talaq', it is because he described it with severity, since 'af'al' is intended for description, therefore it does not indicate three without intention because the superlative 'af'al' is part of what it is added to, so 'ashadd' is an expression for the source which is divorce. As for 'ka-alf', the similitude can be in strength or in number; if he intended the second, three occur; if not, the lesser (irrevocability) is established. Included in this is 'like a thousand' and 'like three' and 'one like a thousand'; except in these, if he intends three, only one falls, by agreement, because one cannot carry three. This is in al-Jawhara. Excluded from it is 'like the number of a thousand' and 'like the number of three', because these result in three without intention. Also included is if he likens to something without number, like the number of the sun or dust, or says 'like it', because similitude necessitates some kind of increase, and that exists in irrevocability. With the assumption of the verbal noun, as in 'al-battah', it is in the meaning of 'divorce absolutely', and similarly in 'afhash al-talaq' it is in the meaning of 'a most repulsive divorce'. And so on for the rest. His saying: (with the letter tha (ث)) and as for what is in the text of al-Tanwir with the letter ta (ت) from above, the correct is with tha, as al-Ramli noted in his glosses on al-Minah, and said that the ruling is correct in that as well, and he mentioned in his fatawa and similar, and gave fatwa with three in that also. I say: It can be answered that he intended to alert that the expression with tha is more appropriate; consider his saying: (it does not occur immediately until she menstruates or he has intercourse with her in that purity). He said in al-Nahr:
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) by the author of the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq. It explains that when a husband uses descriptive phrases like 'ba'in' (irrevocable), 'al-battah' (absolutely), 'afhash al-talaq' (most repulsive divorce), etc., and does not intend a specific number, the divorce falls as one ba'in. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that these phrases describe the divorce as having the quality of irrevocability (baynuna). The author addresses an objection: if these words carry the possibility of irrevocability, why can't a mere 'taliq' also be intended as ba'in? The answer is that intention acts on the uttered word; in 'taliq ba'in', the word 'ba'in' is actually spoken, whereas in just 'taliq' the word 'ba'in' is not uttered. The author notes a point of critique from Fath al-Qadir regarding this. He then discusses the condition that 'ba'in' must be an adjective without conjunction; if joined with 'and' or 'then', different rulings apply. He further explores exceptions: if the husband intends two by using separate statements, two ba'in divorces can fall. He also explains each phrase individually: 'afhash al-talaq' includes all superlative descriptors except 'akthar' (most numerous) which indicates three. For 'talaq al-shaytan' and 'talaq al-bid'a', he clarifies that they result in one ba'in because raj'i is usually sunni, but here it's not. He reconciles a previous ruling about bid'a divorce not falling immediately in certain conditions. For 'ka-al-jabal' (like a mountain), the similitude indicates an increase in the quality (irrevocability). For 'ka-alf' (like a thousand), similitude can be in strength (leading to one ba'in) or in number (leading to three if intended). Finally, he addresses a textual variant in al-Tanwir where 'akthar' is written with ta instead of tha, noting that the ruling of three applies in both readings. The passage ends mid-sentence on page 501.

Key Terms
قوله (qawluhoo): Here it refers to the author of the matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq), as the commentator quotes his words.
البتة (al-battah): An absolute or irrevocable divorce; it is a source (masdar) used adverbially, meaning 'absolutely'.
أفحش الطلاق (afhash al-talaq): The most repulsive divorce; a superlative form that describes the divorce as repulsive.
البينونة (al-baynuna): Irrevocability; the state of being ba'in, meaning the husband cannot take her back without a new marriage.
التشبيه (al-tashbih): Similitude; comparing the divorce to something else (like a mountain, a thousand) to convey a quality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his wife 'anti taliq ba'in' with no intention of any number. How many divorces occur according to the Hanafi school?
A) Three divorces
B) One ba'in divorce
C) One raj'i divorce
D) Two ba'in divorces

Key Takeaway
Descriptive divorce phrases like 'ba'in' yield one ba'in divorce unless the husband intends three; 'akthar' is the only exception that gives three without intention.

Descriptive Similes in Talaq: Ba'in vs. Three
Explaining when phrases like 'afhash al-talaq' yield ba’in or three divorces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on descriptive superlative phrases in divorce (e.g., afhash al-talaq) and distinguish between quality-based and number-based expressions.

Core Idea
A phrase like 'afhash al-talaq' gives one ba’in, not three—superlatives describe quality, not quantity.

When a husband uses exaggerated language like 'the most obscene divorce,' it might seem to demand multiple divorces. But Hanafi fiqh carefully distinguishes between describing the quality of a divorce and specifying its number. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and legal reasoning behind the rule that descriptive similes yield one irrevocable (ba’in) divorce unless the wording explicitly refers to count. We will examine the passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq where the author details the effects of various superlative expressions and similes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase type
Read the divorce statement and determine whether it uses a descriptive superlative (af‘al al-tafdil) like 'afhash' or a numerical phrase like 'aktharuh' (with tha). The ruling hinges on this distinction.
Descriptive superlatives modify the quality of the divorce; numerical phrases specify quantity.
2
Apply the Hanafi rule for af‘al al-tafdil
Recall that af‘al al-tafdil indicates variation in quality, not number. Since the worst quality of divorce is irrevocable (ba’in), the phrase 'afhash' and its synonyms yield ba’in, not three.
The author states: 'af‘al al-tafdil is part of what it is added to'—so it describes the divorce itself.
3
Check for intention (niyyah)
If the husband intended three, his intention is considered only if the phrase is ambiguous (e.g., 'ka-alf'). For explicit descriptive superlatives, intention does not change the outcome; they produce ba’in regardless.
For 'aktharuh', however, intention is not accepted—three occur even if he intended one.
4
Determine the legal outcome
Based on the phrase type and any relevant intention, conclude whether the divorce is one ba’in, three, or something else. Use the passage’s examples: 'afhash' → ba’in; 'aktharuh' → three; 'ka-alf' with intention of number → three, else ba’in.
The passage also covers exceptions like 'ka-‘adad al-alf' which always gives three.

It is revocable, and if he mentioned with the letter fa and the rest remains as is, then it is irrevocable—thus in al-Dhakhira. And his statement 'so it is one' if he did not intend three indicates that if he intended two it is not valid because it is a pure number, except if he meant: 'You are divorced one' and with his statement 'irrevocable' or 'al-batta' or similar another, two divorces occur based on the construction being predication after predication, and they are both irrevocable because the irrevocability of the first necessitates the irrevocability of the second, since the meaning of revocable is that it is such that he can take her back, and that is negated by the connection of the second irrevocable, so there is no benefit in describing it as revocable. And every kinaya coupled with talaq follows this, so two irrevocable occur. And he indicated by 'afhash al-talaq' every description on af‘al because it denotes variation, and that is achieved by irrevocability, and it is worse than revocable divorce. I say: There is no contradiction between them because what they mentioned here is the occurrence of one ba'in without intention, regardless of whether it occurs immediately or after something exists. And he indicated by 'ka-al-jabal' the simile that necessitates increase in greatness, and it is by increase of the description of irrevocability, so enters like 'jabal'. As for ba'in with 'ashadd al-talaq', because he described it with severity, because af‘al is intended for description, so it is not for three without intention because af‘al al-tafdil is part of what it is added to, so 'ashadd' expresses the source which is talaq. Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq discusses the rulings on descriptive similes in divorce statements. The author addresses phrases like 'afhash al-talaq' (most obscene divorce) and similar expressions using the pattern af‘al (superlative). The key ruling: such phrases generally result in a single irrevocable (ba’in) divorce, not three, unless the phrase explicitly indicates number (such as 'aktharuh' with tha, meaning 'most in number', which yields three). The reasoning is that af‘al denotes variation in quality, and the worst quality in divorce is irrevocability (ba’in) as opposed to revocable (raj‘i). However, if the phrase is ambiguous like 'ka-alf' (like a thousand), it can mean either strength or number; if no intention, the lesser (ba’in) is presumed. The passage also distinguishes between wording that is purely descriptive vs. wording that mentions number explicitly (e.g., 'ka-‘adad al-alf'), which yields three even without intention. The author harmonizes this with earlier rulings on bid‘i divorce, noting that 'talaq al-shaytan' and 'talaq al-bid‘a' also result in ba’in because they are associated with bid‘a rather than sunna, even if the divorce itself is revocable in some cases. Overall, the rule is that descriptive similes produce ba’in, while numerical similes produce three if they explicitly refer to number.

Key Terms
أفحش الطلاق (afhash al-talaq): The most obscene divorce—a descriptive phrase using the superlative pattern af‘al.
بائن (ba’in): Irrevocable divorce—the husband cannot take her back without a new marriage.
أفعل التفضيل (af‘al al-tafdil): The superlative pattern used to describe a quality of the divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) No divorce occurs because the phrase is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, descriptive superlative phrases in talaq (e.g., afhash al-talaq) yield one ba’in divorce; only phrases that explicitly refer to number (e.g., aktharuh) produce three divorces.

Ruling on 'You are divorced like the stars'
Ruling on talaq phrases with comparisons and number additions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for talaq phrases involving comparison to stars or addition to numbers, and apply the conditions regarding existence of the counted items.

Core Idea
Saying 'like the stars' without number means one divorce; adding an impossible number nullifies the count.

Divorce (talaq) can be expressed in various phrases. This lesson clarifies a specific set: when a husband compares the divorce to stars or attaches an impossible number. Understanding the Hanafi ruling helps avoid confusion about the number of divorces and ensures correct application in real scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase type
Determine whether the husband's statement is a comparison (e.g., 'like the stars') or an addition to a number (e.g., 'like the number of stars').
The comparison focuses on quality, the addition on quantity.
2
Check if number is specified
If no number is mentioned, the default ruling is one divorce. If a number is specified, move to the next step.
The word 'number' (عدد) triggers the quantitative ruling.
3
Analyze the nature of the counted items
Classify the added number into one of three categories: known to be non-existent (e.g., hair on palm), unknown in existence (e.g., hair of Iblis), or temporarily absent (e.g., shaved leg hair).
The existence condition at the time of utterance is crucial.
4
Apply the ruling per category
For impossible or unknown numbers, the number is void and one divorce occurs. For temporarily absent items, no divorce occurs due to missing condition.
If the number is valid and possible, it results in multiple divorces (typically three).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية : لو قال أنت طالق كالنجوم فهي واحدة يعني كالنجوم ضياء لا عدداً إلا أن يقول كعدد النجوم، ولو أضافه إلى عدد معلوم النفي كعدد شعر بطن كفي، أو مجهول النفي والإثبات كعدد شعر إبليس أو نحوه وقعت واحدة أو من شأنه الثبوت لكنه كان زائلاً وقت الحلف بعارض كعدد شعر ساقي أو ساقك وقد تنور لا يقع لعدم الشرط. كذا في كافي الحاكم.
And in al-Zahiriyya: If he said 'You are divorced like the stars', then it is one, meaning like the stars in light not number, unless he says 'like the number of stars'. And if he added it to a number known to be non-existent, like 'the number of hair on the palm of my hand', or unknown in existence and non-existence like 'the number of hair of Iblis' or similar, then one occurs. Or if it is of a nature to be existent but was absent at the time of oath due to a temporary cause, like 'the number of hair on my shin' or 'your shin while it is shaved', it does not occur due to non-existence of the condition. Thus in Kafi al-Hakim.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage from al-Zahiriyya presents the ruling for various phrases where a husband compares his wife's divorce to stars or adds a number. The key principle: 'like the stars' without 'number' means one divorce (like stars in radiance, not count). If he says 'like the number of stars', it implies multiplicity and causes three divorces for a consummated marriage. The passage also covers cases where the number is added to something impossible (like hair on palm, which doesn't exist) or something unknown (like hair of Iblis) – in such cases, one divorce occurs because the number is void. If the number refers to something that usually exists but is temporarily removed (like shaved leg hair), no divorce occurs because the condition (existence of the counted items) is absent. This is a clear ruling of the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الظهيرية (al-Zahiriyya): A Hanafi fiqh work by al-Zahiri (or a compilation attributed to him), often cited for rare transmission.

Key Takeaway
Saying 'like the stars' without number means one divorce; specifying a known possible number can lead to three; impossible or absent numbers result in one.

Ruling on 'You are forbidden to me a thousand times'
Ruling on repeated prohibition phrase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for the phrase 'أنت علي حرام ألف مرة' and explain why only one divorce takes effect.

Core Idea
Repeating the word 'haram' multiple times does not multiply divorces — only one divorce occurs even for a thousand repetitions.

This passage from al-Bazzaziyya addresses a specific scenario: a husband says to his wife, 'You are forbidden to me a thousand times.' The text clearly states that only one divorce takes effect. This ruling is based on the principle that once the first prohibition triggers a divorce (for a consummated wife), she becomes irrevocably divorced, and subsequent repetitions have no effect. However, if the intention is to divorce three times with a clear expression, that is treated differently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its legal context
The phrase is 'أنت علي حرام ألف مرة'. It is a statement of prohibition (haram) repeated numerously.
The phrase is typically used in anger or exaggeration.
2
Recall the baseline ruling for 'You are forbidden to me'
In Hanafi fiqh, this phrase causes one divorce (bain) if said to a wife in a consummated marriage, provided no intention for more.
If the marriage is not consummated, the divorce is not effective – only one divorce occurs upon consummation.
3
Apply the principle of repetition
Repeating the word 'haram' multiple times does not create multiple divorces because the first occurrence already makes the woman bain (irrevocably divorced), and subsequent repetitions are on a divorced woman, thus ineffective.
This is based on the rule that a divorced woman cannot be divorced again until she marries another.
4
Note the exception for explicit intention
If the husband explicitly intends three divorces by saying it three times in one sentence, then three occur (as per other rulings, but not directly from this short text).
The exception is based on intention and wording, not on numerical repetition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البزازية : أنت علي حرام ألف مرة تقع واحدة ا هـ.
And in al-Bazzaziyya: 'You are forbidden to me a thousand times' – one divorce occurs. End quote.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This short text from al-Bazzaziyya provides a clear ruling: if a husband says to his wife 'You are forbidden to me a thousand times', only one divorce takes effect. The reasoning (discussed in the commentary that follows in the original text) is that the phrase 'a thousand times' is like repeating the word 'haram' multiple times. Once the first prohibition causes a divorce (for a consummated wife), she becomes a bain (irrevocable) and the subsequent repetitions fall on a divorced woman, thus not effective. However, if he intends three divorces by saying 'You are forbidden to me' three times in one sentence, then three occur. The author later clarifies the distinction.

Key Terms
البزازية (al-Bazzaziyya): A later Hanafi fatwa collection by al-Bazzazi.

D) No divorce.

Key Takeaway
When a husband says 'You are forbidden to me' repeatedly, only one divorce occurs because the woman becomes irrevocable after the first prohibition.

Ruling on Divorce by a Non-Existent Number
Ruling on divorce conditional on a non-existent number

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a husband conditions divorce on a non-existent number and explain why one divorce occurs according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A conditional number that does not exist renders the condition meaningless; one divorce takes effect.

This short ruling from al-Zahiriyya addresses a specific case where a husband utters a conditional divorce that cannot be fulfilled. The principle at play is that if the condition is impossible or refers to a non-existent quantity, the divorce is treated as a simple, unconditional pronouncement. Understanding this helps clarify the Hanafi approach to conditional divorce and its practical applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional phrase
Locate the condition in the husband's statement: 'by the number of fish in this basin'.
The number is tied to a specific basin.
2
Determine if the condition is possible
Check if the number can be known. Here, the basin has no fish, so the number is zero – effectively non-existent.
A condition that refers to a non-existent quantity is impossible to fulfill.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle: impossible condition renders divorce unconditional
In Hanafi fiqh, if a condition attached to divorce is impossible, the condition is void and the divorce is treated as an unconditional pronouncement.
This principle is derived from the maxim: 'What is impossible is treated as if it were not mentioned.'
4
State the consequence: one divorce occurs
Since the condition is void, only one divorce takes effect, regardless of the wording about number.
The text explicitly says 'تقع واحدة' – one divorce occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage gives a ruling for a case where the husband conditions divorce on the number of something that does not exist (empty basin). Since the number is of non-existent items, it is akin to saying 'you are divorced by the number of nothing', and therefore only one divorce takes effect. This is similar to the earlier ruling about hair on the palm.

Key Terms

تقع (taqa'): Occurs, takes effect. Specifically, the divorce occurs.
ظهيرية (al-Zahiriyya): A Hanafi legal work by Imam al-Zahiriyya, used as a source for rulings.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Conditional Divorce When the Condition Relies on Non-Existent Objects
Intra-Hanafi dispute on divorce conditional on a number when the counted items are absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between the positions of Imam Muhammad and al-Karkhi on divorce conditional on a number of hair when the hair is absent, and apply the underlying principle to analogous cases.

Core Idea
When divorce is conditional on a number, the existence of the counted items matters: Muhammad requires existence; Karkhi counts the number anyway.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq narrates an intra-Hanafi debate. The case involves a man who said to his shaved wife 'You are divorced by the number of your pubic hair.' Imam Muhammad likens the situation to the back of the hand (where hair normally grows) and the palm (where it does not). Al-Karkhi disagrees, holding that the number itself is legally considered even if the counted items are absent. The dispute reveals a deeper principle about the interpretation of conditional statements involving numbers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core case
The husband says 'You are divorced by the number of hair on your pubic area' after the wife has shaved it. The hair no longer exists at the time of the statement.
2
Understand Muhammad's analogy
Muhammad compares the pubic hair to two examples: the back of the hand (where hair normally grows, but shaved) and the palm (where hair never grows). This analogy helps determine the legal effect.
3
Derive Muhammad's ruling
For the back of hand (hair normally present but shaved), the condition requires actual existence of the counted hair; since none exist, the condition fails, but the divorce still occurs (one). For the palm (where hair never exists), the condition is impossible from the start, and one divorce also occurs.
The text indicates one divorce occurs in both scenarios.
4
Contrast with al-Karkhi's ruling
Al-Karkhi holds that regardless of whether the hair exists or not, the number itself is legally considered. Thus, three divorces occur in all cases.
5
Apply the underlying principle
The dispute hinges on whether a condition contingent on a number requires the counted items to exist or merely the number to be conceptually valid. This principle extends to other similar cases, like the broth example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحكى ابن عن محمد قال : كنا عند محمد بن الحسن فسئل عمن قال لامرأته أنت طالق عدد الشعر الذي على فرجك وقد كانت أطلت، فبقي محمد بن الحسن يتفكر فيه وشبهه بظهر الكف ثم أجمع رأيه على أنه إن قال أنت طالق بعدد الشعر الذي على ظهر كفي وقد أطلي أنه يقع، وإن قال بعدد الشعر الذي في بطن كفي أنه يقع واحدة لأنه في الأول يقع على عدد الشعور النابتة، فإذا لم يكن عليه شعر لم يوجد الشرط، وفي الثانية لا يقع على عدد الشعر. وذكر الكرخي أنها تطلق ثلاثاً في عدد شعر رأسي أو عدد شعر ظهر كفي وقد أطلي لأنه ذو عدد وإن لم يكن موجوداً، ولو قال أنت طالق عدد ما في هذه القصعة من النريد إن قال صب المرقة عليه فهي ثلاث وإن قال بعد صب المرقة فهي واحدة ا هـ.
And [Ibn...] narrated from Muhammad, he said: We were with Muhammad ibn al-Hasan when he was asked about a man who said to his wife 'You are divorced by the number of hair on your pubic area' while she had shaved it. Muhammad ibn al-Hasan reflected on it and likened it to the back of the hand, then his opinion resolved that if he said 'You are divorced by the number of hair on the back of my hand while I have shaved it', it occurs, but if he said 'by the number of hair in the palm of my hand', it occurs one, because in the first case it is upon the number of growing hair, so if there is no hair the condition is not met, and in the second case it does not fall upon the number of hair. And al-Karkhi mentioned that she is divorced three times by the number of hair on my head or the number of hair on the back of my hand while shaved, because it is a number even if not existent; and if he said 'You are divorced by the number of what is in this bowl of broth' - if he said (the phrase) before pouring the broth on it, it is three, and if after pouring the broth, it is one.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding divorce conditional on the number of hair when the hair has been removed. Muhammad ibn al-Hasan (Imam Muhammad) distinguishes between two cases: if he refers to hair that usually grows (back of hand) and it is shaved, the divorce does not occur because the counted items are absent; if he refers to hair that is not naturally found (palm), the condition is impossible, so one divorce occurs. Al-Karkhi disagrees, ruling that in both cases (head hair or back of hand hair, even if shaved), three divorces occur because the number itself is legally considered (like counting), even if the counted items are not present. The Karkhi also distinguishes for the broth case: if he says it before pouring, the broth exists, so three; after pouring, it is gone, so one. The text does not give a final preference, but later authorities may follow Muhammad's view.

Key Terms
ظهر الكف (zahr al-kaff): The back of the hand, where hair normally grows.
بطن الكف (batn al-kaff): The palm of the hand, where hair does not grow naturally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife 'You are divorced by the number of hair on the back of my hand' after he has shaved it. According to Muhammad, how many divorces occur?
A) No divorce.
B) One divorce.
C) Three divorces.
D) The intention of the husband matters.

Key Takeaway
In conditional divorce involving a number, the existence of the counted items is crucial: Imam Muhammad requires actual existence; al-Karkhi counts the number irrespective.

Default Revocable in Bid'a Divorce; Intention Can Override
Ruling on bid'a divorce and role of intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default ruling for a divorce described as 'for innovation' (bid'a) and explain when the husband's intention changes the outcome to an irrevocable divorce.

Core Idea
Bid'a in divorce creates doubt; default is revocable unless intention specifies irrevocable.

When a husband says 'You are divorced for innovation,' the ambiguity of the term 'bid'a' leads to a default ruling of one revocable divorce, because the least burdensome effect is presumed. However, if the husband intended an irrevocable divorce, his intention is considered, and the divorce becomes irrevocable. This lesson examines the reasoning behind these rules and the intra-Hanafi nuance reported from Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance and its ambiguity
The phrase 'for innovation' (bid'a) can refer to an irrevocable divorce or to divorce during menstruation (which is innovated but revocable). This ambiguity triggers the principle that doubt prevents establishing the stricter ruling (irrevocability).
The same applies to 'divorce of Satan.'
2
Determine the default ruling
Due to the ambiguity, the default ruling from al-Bada'i is one revocable divorce. The doubt about whether the divorce is irrevocable is resolved by presuming the least effect.
This is a standard Hanafi principle: al-yaqīn lā yazūlu bi al-shakk.
3
Consider the exception: intention matters
Abu Yusuf holds that if the husband intended one irrevocable divorce when uttering the phrase, it becomes irrevocable. His reasoning: the wording 'for innovation' can contextually describe the type of divorce, so the intention clarifies the ambiguity.
This view is narrated from Abu Yusuf and represents a permissible interpretive option.
4
Identify the practical difference
The khilāf (difference) affects the marital status: under the default view, the wife remains in revocable divorce (can return without new marriage); under Abu Yusuf's view, if intention was irrevocable, the divorce is bā'in (requires new marriage for reunion).
The muftā bihī (operative ruling) is not stated; a mufti would likely prefer the default unless strong evidence of intention exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلت : وفي البدائع من هذا الباب ولو قال لها أنت طالق للبدعة فهي واحدة رجعية لأن البدعة قد تكون في البائن وقد تكون في الطلاق في حالة الحيض فيقع الشك في ثبوت البينونة فلا يثبت بالشك، وكذا إذا قال أنت طالق طلاق الشيطان فهو كقوله أنت طالق للبدعة، وروي عن أبي يوسف فيمن قال لامرأته أنت طالق للبدعة ونوى واحدة بائنة فهي واحدة بائنة لأن لفظه يحتمل ذلك على ما بينا فصحت نيته . اهـ. تأمل.
I said: In al-Bada'i from this chapter: If he said to her 'You are divorced for innovation', it is one revocable, because innovation (bid'a) may occur in irrevocable divorce and may occur in divorce during menstruation, so doubt arises about the establishment of irrevocability, and it is not established by doubt. Similarly, if he said 'You are divorced with the divorce of Satan', it is like his saying 'You are divorced for innovation'. And it is narrated from Abu Yusuf regarding one who said to his wife 'You are divorced for innovation' and intended one irrevocable, it is one irrevocable because his wording can bear that as we explained, so his intention is valid. End.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of divorce when described as 'for innovation' (bid'a). The author first mentions the view from al-Bada'i: such a divorce is one revocable (raj'i) because the term 'bid'a' is ambiguous—it could refer to an irrevocable divorce (bain) or a divorce during menstruation. Since the intention is unclear, the default is the least effect: one revocable. The same applies to 'divorce of Satan'. However, a narration from Abu Yusuf says that if the husband intended a single irrevocable divorce, it becomes one irrevocable because the wording 'for innovation' can be understood as describing the type of divorce itself. The text ends with 'tammal' implying reflection.

Key Terms
البدعة (al-bid'a): In this context, divorce that is contrary to the Sunnah, e.g., during menstruation.
رجعية (raj'iyya): Revocable divorce where the husband can take his wife back during the waiting period without a new marriage.
بائنة (bā'ina): Irrevocable divorce; the wife becomes forbidden to the husband until she marries another and is divorced (in three-divorce cases) or requires a new marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says 'You are divorced for innovation' and it is proven that he intended one irrevocable divorce. According to Abu Yusuf, what is the ruling?
A) One revocable divorce.
B) One irrevocable divorce.
C) Three divorces.
D) No divorce because the phrase is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
In bid'a divorce, the default is one revocable; intention can override only if the husband intended irrevocability.

Ruling on 'Divorced by Number of Dust' — Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on divorce formula using uncountable vs countable nouns

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the number of divorces in the 'dust' case and explain the reasoning based on countability.

Core Idea
Dust is uncountable, so one divorce; sand is countable, so three.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute about a divorce formula involving the number of dust particles versus sand grains. The key distinction lies in the linguistic nature of these nouns: 'dust' is a collective uncountable mass, while 'sand' is countable as individual grains. This leads to different rulings by two great imams of the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the formula
Recognize the two phrases: 'by the number of dust' and 'by the number of sand'. Note that they are used as expressions of multiplicity in divorce pronouncement.
The husband's intention may be to indicate a large number, but the linguistic nature of the noun matters.
2
Note the ikhtilaf
For 'dust', Abu Yusuf rules only one divorce (because dust is not countable), while Muhammad rules three (because the expression implies exaggeration and multiplicity). For 'sand', there is consensus on three divorces since sand can be counted.
The disagreement is within the Hanafi school itself, making this an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Understand Abu Yusuf's dalil
Abu Yusuf considers 'dust' a collective noun that does not denote countable units. Therefore, the phrase 'by the number of dust' does not indicate a specific number; the default is one divorce (the minimum).
This relies on the principle that ambiguous expressions in talaq are interpreted with the least effect unless intention is clear.
4
Understand Muhammad's dalil
Muhammad argues that the expression is used for exaggeration, similar to saying 'countless times', so it implies a large number. In the context of talaq, the maximum valid talaq is three, so three divorces occur.
Muhammad prioritizes the intended meaning of hyperbole over the literal countability.
5
Apply the principle of default minimum
The passage also discusses the phrase 'fill of the house', which can mean either a single large object or many small ones. In absence of intention, the lesser effect (one) is established. This mirrors the default rule for ambiguous expressions.
This principle (al-yaqeen la yazalu bi al-shakk) ensures certainty in the divorce count.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعرضها كذا الجوهرة بين التراب والرمل فقال : لو قال أنت طالق عدد التراب فهي واحدة عند أبي يوسف، وثلاث عند محمد . وإن قال عدد الرمل فهي ثلاث إجماعاً، وأما البينونة بملء البيت فلأن الشيء قد يملأ البيت لعظمه في نفسه وقد يملؤه لكثرته فأيهما نوى صحت نيته، وعند عدمها يثبت الأقل.
And '...' such: Al-Jawhara (?) between dust and sand said: If he says 'You are divorced by the number of dust', then it is one according to Abu Yusuf, and three according to Muhammad. And if he says 'by the number of sand', it is three by consensus. As for irrevocability by 'fill of the house', it is because something may fill the house due to its own greatness or due to its quantity, so whichever he intended, his intention is valid, and in absence of intention, the least is established.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage discusses the divorce conditional on the number of dust (turab) vs sand (raml). Dust is considered a collective noun not countable, so Abu Yusuf rules one divorce, while Muhammad says three because the intention is to indicate multiplicity. Sand is countable, so there is consensus on three divorces. The passage then transitions to the phrase 'fill of the house': it can mean a single large thing or many things; the husband's intention determines the outcome, otherwise the minimum (one) applies. This shows the principle that ambiguous phrases are interpreted in the least effect unless intention is clear.

Key Terms
التراب (al-turab): Dust, considered as a non-countable mass.
الرمل (al-raml): Sand, considered countable as individual grains.
ملء البيت (mal' al-bayt): Fill of the house; an ambiguous phrase meaning either a single large object or a collection of many small ones.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) None.

Key Takeaway
The countability of the noun in a divorce formula determines whether the expression implies one or three divorces in Hanafi law.

Ruling on Describing Divorce with Severe, Long, or Wide
Ruling on describing talaq with attributes indicating intensity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when describing a divorce as severe, long, or wide makes it irrevocable (bain) and when it remains revocable (raj'i) based on Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
An attribute of divorce may render it irrevocable, but the attribute must qualify the divorce, not the woman.

In Hanafi fiqh, the words used in divorce affect its legal consequences. A husband's description of the divorce as 'severe', 'long', or 'wide' can make it irrevocable (bain). However, the critical condition is that the attribute must be attached to the word 'divorce', not to the woman. This passage from Bahar al-Raiq clarifies the conditions and nuances, distinguishing between cases where the description leads to bain versus raj'i. Understanding these rules helps in correctly classifying the divorce type and avoiding mistakes in marital dissolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key attributes
Recognize the attributes: shadid (severe), tawil (long), arid (wide). Understand that these imply magnitude or difficulty, which in Hanafi law makes a divorce irrevocable (bain) because the woman cannot be taken back without a new marriage.
2
Apply the condition of attribute placement
The attribute must directly describe the divorce (taliqa). If the husband says 'You are divorced severe' (without the word 'divorce' explicitly attached to the attribute), the attribute could describe the woman (since feminine), so the default is revocable (raj'i) unless he intended bain.
This is because the word 'shadid' in Arabic can be an adjective of the woman (she is severe).
3
Handle length and width mentioned together
If the husband says 'You are divorced length such and width such', it is one bain divorce, not three, even if he intended three. The text states that length and width indicate strength but are attributed to a single thing, so the intention of three is invalid.
As if he said 'one divorce whose length is such'.
4
Distinguish between intended and default meanings
When the husband's expression is ambiguous, the default ruling is the least effect (revocable) unless he explicitly intended otherwise. Intention can be considered if the wording allows it, but not if the wording clearly indicates a specific type (e.g., length/width with divorce indicates one bain).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبينونة بملء البيت فلأن الشيء قد يملأ البيت لعظمه في نفسه وقد يملؤه لكثرته فأيهما نوى صحت نيته، وعند عدمها يثبت الأقل. وأما البينونة بتطليقة شديدة وما بعده فلأن ما لا يمكن تداركه يشتد عليه وهو البائن، وما يصعب تداركه يقال فيه لهذا الأمر طول وعرض فهو البائن أيضاً. قيد بكون الشدة وأخواتها صفة للتطليقة لأنه لو قال أنت طالق قوية أو شديدة أو طويلة أو عريضة ولم يذكر التطليقة كان رجعياً لأنه لا يصلح أن يكون صفة للطلاق، ويصلح أن يكون صفة للمرأة كما ذكره الأسبيجابي. وقيد بقوله طويلة أو عريضة لأنه لو قال أنت طالق طول كذا وعرض كذا فهي واحدة بائنة ولا تكون ثلاثاً وإن نواها لأن الطول والعرض يدلان على القوة لكنهما يكونان للشيء الواحد، وكأنه قال طالق واحدة طولها كذا فلم تصح نية الثلاث. كذا في كافي الحاكم.
And irrevocability by 'fill of the house' is because something may fill the house due to its own greatness or due to its quantity, so whichever he intended, his intention is valid, and in absence of it, the least is established. As for irrevocability with 'a severe divorce' and beyond, it is because what cannot be repaired is severe (shadid) and that is the irrevocable divorce, and what is difficult to repair is said to have length and width, so that is also irrevocable. It is conditioned that severity and its likes be attributes of the divorce (taliqa) because if he said 'You are divorced strong' or 'severe' or 'long' or 'wide' and did not mention the divorce, it would be revocable, because these do not qualify as descriptions of the divorce, but can be descriptions of the woman, as mentioned by al-Asbijabi. And it is conditioned that he says long or wide because if he said 'You are divorced length so-and-so and width so-and-so', it is one irrevocable and not three even if he intended three, because length and width indicate strength but they are for a single thing, as if he said 'one divorce whose length is such', so the intention of three is not valid. Thus in Kafi al-Hakim.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage discusses when describing a divorce with attributes like 'severe', 'long', 'wide' makes it irrevocable (bain). The key condition: the attribute must describe the divorce itself (taliqa), not the woman. If he says 'You are divorced with a severe divorce', it is bain because severity implies irreversibility. Similarly, 'long' or 'wide' imply magnitude, also bain. However, if he omits the word 'taliqa' and says 'You are divorced severe', the attribute could refer to the woman, so the default is revocable (raj'i) unless intention is for bain. Also, if he says 'length such and width such', it is one bain, not three, because the description fits a single divorce. The author cites al-Asbijabi and Kafi al-Hakim.

Key Terms
البائن (al-ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that cannot be revoked except through a new marriage.
الرجعي (al-raj'i): A revocable divorce where the husband can take back his wife during the waiting period without a new marriage.
شدة (shidda): Severity, used as an attribute of divorce indicating irrevocability.
طول وعرض (tul wa 'ard): Length and width, used metaphorically to indicate magnitude or difficulty of repair, making the divorce bain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One revocable raj'i divorce
B) One irrevocable bain divorce
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
The irrevocability of a described divorce hinges on whether the attribute directly qualifies the divorce act, not the woman, and if length/width are used, it is one bain.

Ruling on describing divorce as 'best' or 'most perfect'
Ruling on describing talaq with virtuous attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a husband describes divorce with virtuous attributes and distinguish it from descriptions that indicate increase.

Core Idea
Virtuous descriptions like 'best divorce' do not change the revocable nature of a single divorce.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a common misconception about talaq. When a husband says 'You are divorced with the best divorce' or similar virtuous descriptions, the description itself has no legal effect on the type of divorce. The divorce remains revocable (raj'i) unless the husband intended three. However, if the description indicates an increase—like likening the divorce to something tiny—it becomes irrevocable. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its description
Examine the husband's pronouncement. The passage lists various virtuous attributes such as 'best', 'most perfect', 'most just', etc. Note that these are commendatory but do not imply any numerical or qualitative increase in the divorce's effect.
Examples: أحسن الطلاق, أسنه, أجمله, etc.
2
Determine the default ruling
According to the passage, when the description does not indicate increase, the divorce falls as one revocable (raj'i) divorce that is sunni (properly timed). This is the default in the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya).
فيقع رجعياً وتكون طالقاً للسنة في وقتها.
3
Check for intention of three
If the husband intended three divorces when uttering the virtuous description, then it becomes three sunni divorces. The intention is a separate factor.
وإن نوى ثلاثاً فهي ثلاث للسنة.
4
Recognize the exception for hyperbolic descriptions
Descriptions that indicate an increase in the effect—such as comparing the divorce to a tiny object like a mustard seed—make the divorce irrevocable (ba'in). The passage explicitly states this exception.
الوصف بما ينبئ عن الزيادة يوجب البينونة.
5
Understand the authority cited
The author references al-Hakim's Kafi and al-Asbijabi to confirm the ruling. Note that the position is based on zahir al-riwaya, the most authoritative transmission in the Hanafi school.
كذا في كافي الحاكم. وذكر الأسبيجابي أنها تكون رجعية في ظاهر الرواية.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it is conditioned as mentioned in the descriptions because if he describes it with something that cannot be described, the description is ignored and it falls as revocable, like 'a divorce that does not fall upon you' or 'and I have the option'. And if it can be described but does not indicate an increase in its effect, like his saying 'the best divorce', 'the most suitable', 'the most beautiful', 'the most just', 'the last of it', 'the most perfect', 'the best of it', 'the most complete' - it falls as revocable, and she is divorced according to the Sunnah in its time. And if he intended three, it is three according to the Sunnah. Thus in Kafi al-Hakim. Commentary
This passage covers the ruling when the husband describes the divorce with virtuous attributes like 'best', 'most perfect', etc. These descriptions do not affect the legal type of divorce; it remains revocable (raj'i) and counts as a sunni divorce (i.e., properly timed). However, if he intended three divorces, it becomes three (all sunni). The author cites Kafi al-Hakim and al-Asbijabi, noting that the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya) says it is revocable. Then the passage concludes that a description indicating increase (like comparison to a tiny thing) makes it irrevocable (bain). This shows the subtle distinction between attributes that are purely commendatory and those that express exaggeration.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The most authoritative transmissions in the Hanafi school, i.e., the six books of Imam Muhammad.
رجعي (raj'i): Revocable divorce during the waiting period.
بائن (ba'in): Irrevocable divorce; husband cannot take back during 'iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Three.
D) None.

Key Takeaway
Virtuous descriptions like 'best divorce' do not affect the revocable nature of a single divorce unless they indicate an increase (e.g., hyperbolic comparison) or the husband intended three.

Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Comparison in Divorce: The Tip of a Needle
Ruling on comparison in divorce with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether comparison to a small object like a needle tip makes divorce bain (irrevocable), and evaluate the grounds for the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
Comparing divorce to a small thing can imply exaggeration, making it irrevocable — but Hanafi imams differ on the condition.

This lesson examines the effect of tashbih (simile) in divorce pronouncements. The Hanafi school generally treats a comparison as implying increase (ziyada), thereby making the divorce irrevocable (bain). However, the Hanafi imams themselves disagree on the threshold: does every comparison count, or only if the object is inherently great? We will unpack the dispute, focusing on the famous case of 'like the tip of a needle'.

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi principle
Understand that tashbih in talaq is assumed to imply exaggeration (ziyada), making the divorce bain. This is the default ruling.
2
Identify the three positions
Recognize that Imam Abu Hanifa holds any comparison implies increase. Abu Yusuf requires explicit mention of greatness (al-'uzm). Zufar requires the object to be considered great by people.
Muhammad al-Shaybani's position is ambiguous.
3
Apply to the example 'tip of a needle'
For this small object, Abu Hanifa says bain; Abu Yusuf and Zufar say not bain because the object lacks greatness.
The thamarah al-khilaf is clear: the wife's marital status differs.
4
Trace the reasoning
Analyze why the imams differ: they interpret the underlying custom (urf) and the linguistic implication of tashbih differently.
Abu Hanifa focuses on the act of comparison itself, while the others require external consideration.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if a husband uses a small object, the ruling depends on which opinion is followed. This affects whether the wife can remarry without tahlil.
In fatwa, a mufti must be aware of the operative view in his locale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التشبيه فكذلك أي شيء كان المشبه به كرأس إبرة وكحبة خردل وكسمسمة لاقتضاء التشبيه الزيادة. واشترط أبو يوسف ذكر العظم مطلقاً، وزفر أن يكون عظيماً عند الناس، فرأس الإبرة بائن عند الإمام فقط وكالجبل عنده، وعند زفر فقط، وكعظيمة بائن عند الكل وكعظم الإبرة إلا عند زفر، ومحمد: قيل مع الأول، وقيل مع الثاني. وفي البزازية. أنت طالق كالثلج ؛ إن أراد في البرودة فبائن، وإن أراد في البياض
And as for comparison (tashbih), it is the same, whatever the object of comparison, like 'tip of a needle', 'a mustard seed', 'a sesame seed' because comparison necessitates increase. And Abu Yusuf required [mention of] greatness (al-'uzm) absolutely, and Zufar required that it be considered great among people. So 'tip of a needle' is bain only according to the Imam [Abu Hanifa]; and 'like a mountain' is bain according to him and not according to Zufar; and 'great' (azima) is bain according to all except Zufar; and 'like the bone of a needle' except Zufar; and Muhammad: some say with the first, some say with the second. And in al-Bazzaziyya: 'You are divorced like snow'; if he intended coldness, it is bain; if he intended whiteness...
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of comparing the divorce to something small or large. The general principle: comparison implies exaggeration, making it irrevocable (bain). However, there is intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on details. Abu Yusuf demands explicit mention of greatness; Zufar requires that the object be actually considered large by people. Aba Hanifa holds 'tip of a needle' as bain because the comparison itself implies exaggeration. Zufar disagrees. For 'like a mountain', Abu Hanifa says bain, Zufar says not. For 'great' (azima), consensus bain except Zufar. For 'like the bone of a needle', same. Muhammad's position is unclear. Then the text gives a ruling on 'like snow': if he intended coldness (which is a quality of snow that is intense), it is bain; if he intended whiteness (which is not intense), the rule is not completed in the OCRed text. The passage is cut off but provides key ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
التشبيه (al-tashbih): Comparing the divorce to something else, implying multiplicity or intensity.
العظم (al-'uzm): Greatness; Abu Yusuf's condition for comparison to imply increase.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, tashbih in divorce implies increase, making it bain, but the required degree of exaggeration is disputed among the founding imams.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on 'Number of Dust' and 'Number of Sand' in Divorce
Intra-Hanafi dispute on divorce count using 'number of dust' vs 'number of sand'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding divorce by 'number of dust' and identify the linguistic distinction that drives the difference.

Core Idea
When a husband says 'by the number of dust,' the outcome depends on whether 'dust' is counted or not—a linguistic nuance.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of using non-countable nouns in divorce pronouncements. The core issue is linguistic: 'dust' (turab) is a generic singular noun (ism jins ifradi) without a plural, while 'sand' (raml) is a collective noun (ism jins jam'i). This difference determines whether the mention of 'number' is meaningful, leading to varying numbers of divorces among the three Hanafi imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its variants
The husband says 'You are divorced by the number of dust' or 'by the number of sand.' First, distinguish the two nouns and their linguistic categories.
Dust is not countable; sand is countable as a collective.
2
Analyze the linguistic categorization
According to al-Nahr, dust (turab) is ism jins ifradi (generic singular, no plural), while sand (raml) is ism jins jam'i (collective, implies plurality). This determines whether the word 'number' has any effect.
If the noun is non-countable, mentioning 'number' is meaningless.
3
Apply the positions of the three imams
Abu Yusuf: dust not countable → number meaningless → one revocable divorce. Muhammad: 'number' implies abundance → three divorces. Abu Hanifa (via qiyas): likening to an increase → one irrevocable divorce.
For sand, all agree on three divorces because it is countable.
4
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf is the number and type of divorce that occurs. For 'dust,' it can be one revocable, one irrevocable, or three, depending on which imam's view is followed.
In later practice, Muhammad's view often prevails for phrases with 'number.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعرضها كذا الجوهرة بين التراب والرمل فقال : لو قال أنت طالق عدد التراب فهي واحدة عند أبي يوسف، وثلاث عند محمد . وإن قال عدد الرمل فهي ثلاث إجماعاً. قوله : (فهي واحدة عند أبي يوسف أي رجعية كما في الفتح وقال : واختاره إمام الحرمين من الشافعية لأن التشبيه بالعدد فيما لا عدد له لغو ولا عدد للتراب قوله : ( وثلاث عند محمد) قال في الفتح : وهو قول الشافعي وأحمد لأنه يراد بالعدد إذا ذكر الكثرة. وفي قياس قول أبي حنيفة واحدة بائنة لأن التشبيه يقتضي ضرباً من الزيادة كما مر ، ولو قال مثل التراب يقع واحدة رجعية عند محمد . وفي النهر : إنما كان التراب غير معدود لأنه اسم جنس إفرادي بخلاف الرمل فإنه اسم جنس ا هـ .
And he presented it like this: The Jawhara between dust and sand: so if he said, 'You are divorced by the number of dust,' then it is one according to Abu Yusuf, and three according to Muhammad. And if he said, 'by the number of sand,' then it is three by consensus. His statement: ('So it is one according to Abu Yusuf') meaning revocable, as in al-Fath, and he said: and the Imam al-Haramayn of the Shafi'is chose it because likening to number in what has no number is meaningless, and dust has no number. His statement: ('and three according to Muhammad') said in al-Fath: and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad because number, when mentioned, intends abundance. And according to the analogy of Abu Hanifa's saying, it is one irrevocable because likening necessitates a type of increase as previously mentioned, and if he said 'like dust' it falls as one revocable according to Muhammad. And in al-Nahr: dust is not countable because it is a generic singular noun, unlike sand which is a collective genus noun. End.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of using 'number of dust' (عدد التراب) versus 'number of sand' (عدد الرمل) in a divorce pronouncement. The core issue is whether the noun used is countable or not. Abu Yusuf holds that 'dust' is a non-countable generic singular, so likening divorce to its number is meaningless and results in only one revocable divorce (رجعی). Muhammad argues that 'number' implies abundance, so it should produce three divorces. Abu Hanifa's position, via qiyas, is that such likening indicates an increase, making it one irrevocable (بائن) divorce. The text also notes that if one says 'like dust' without 'number', it falls as one revocable according to Muhammad. The distinction is based on linguistics: 'turab' (dust) is اسم جنس إفرادي (a generic singular that does not have a plural), while 'raml' (sand) is اسم جنس جمعي (a collective noun that implies plurality). Hence, 'number of sand' is three by consensus.

Key Terms
رجعی (rajʿī): A revocable divorce where the husband may take back his wife during the 'idda without a new marriage contract.
بائن (bāʾin): An irrevocable divorce where the husband cannot take back his wife without a new marriage contract and consummation with another man (if three).
اسم جنس إفرادي (ism jins ifrādī): A generic singular noun that does not have a plural; it denotes a single species or mass.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on 'number of sand' (عدد الرمل)?
A) One revocable divorce
B) One irrevocable divorce
C) Three divorces by consensus
D) No divorce occurs

Key Takeaway
The countability of the noun used in a divorce pronouncement determines the number of divorces in the Hanafi school.

Filling the House: Intention and Default
Ruling on 'filling the house' description

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a husband says 'you are divorced by filling the house' and explain how intention affects the outcome.

Core Idea
Saying 'filling the house' gives an irrevocable divorce unless intention directs otherwise.

The phrase 'filling the house' (ملء البيت) is a descriptive expression that makes the divorce irrevocable. The Hanafi jurists explain that the phrase may refer to the greatness or abundance of the divorce, and the husband's intention determines which meaning applies. If no intention is present, the fewer (one divorce) is assumed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase
Recognize that 'filling the house' is a descriptive expression used in divorce.
2
Determine possible meanings
The phrase can indicate either greatness (one severe divorce) or abundance (many divorces).
Greatness = one divorce that is significant; abundance = may imply three divorces.
3
Check the husband's intention
If he intended greatness, it is one irrevocable divorce. If he intended abundance, it is multiple (usually three) irrevocable divorces.
Intention is given weight because the phrase is ambiguous.
4
Apply the default
If no intention is established, assume the lesser: one irrevocable divorce.
The lesser is the minimum that fulfills the description.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما البينونة بملء البيت فلأن الشيء قد يملأ البيت لعظمه في نفسه وقد يملؤه لكثرته فأيهما نوى صحت نيته، وعند عدمها يثبت الأقل. وأما البينونة بتطليقة شديدة وما بعده فلأن ما لا يمكن تداركه يشتد عليه وهو البائن، وما يصعب تداركه يقال فيه لهذا الأمر طول وعرض فهو البائن أيضاً. قيد بكون الشدة وأخواتها صفة للتطليقة لأنه لو قال أنت طالق قوية أو شديدة أو طويلة أو عريضة ولم يذكر التطليقة كان رجعياً لأنه لا يصلح أن يكون صفة للطلاق، ويصلح أن يكون صفة للمرأة كما ذكره الأسبيجابي. وقيد بقوله طويلة أو عريضة لأنه لو قال أنت طالق طول كذا وعرض كذا ا فهي واحدة بائنة ولا تكون ثلاثاً وإن نواها لأن الطول والعرض يدلان على القوة لكنهما يكونان للشيء الواحد، وكأنه قال طالق واحدة طولها كذا فلم تصح نية الثلاث. كذا في كافي الحاكم. ولذا صرح بعضهم في شرحه بأن الصحيح أنها لا تقع الثلاث في طويلة أو عريضة وإن نواها ونسبه إلى شمس الأئمة، ورجح بأن النية إنما تعمل في المحتمل وتطليقة بتاء الواحدة لا يحتمل الثلاث وقيد بما ذكر في الأوصاف لأنه لو وصفه بما لا يوصف به يلغو الوصف ويقع رجعياً نحو طلاقاً لا يقع عليك أو على أني بالخيار وإن كان يوصف به ولا ينبئ على زيادة في أثره كقوله أحسن الطلاق أسنه أجمله أعد له أخيره أكمله أفضله أتمه فيقع رجعياً وتكون طالقاً للسنة في وقتها، وإن نوى ثلاثاً فهي ثلاث للسنة. كذا في كافي الحاكم. وذكر الأسبيجابي أنها تكون رجعية في ظاهر الرواية سواء كانت الحالة حالة حيض أو طهر . وذكر ما جزم به الحاكم رواية عن أبي يوسف فصار الحاصل أن الوصف بما ينبئ عن الزيادة يوجب البينونة.
As for irrevocability by 'filling the house,' because a thing may fill the house due to its greatness in itself or due to its abundance; so whichever he intended, his intention is valid, and in the absence of it, the lesser is established. As for irrevocability by a 'strong divorce' and what follows, because what cannot be remedied is severe for him, and that is the irrevocable; and what is difficult to remedy is said about it 'this matter has length and width,' so it is also irrevocable. It is conditioned that 'severity' and its sisters be attributes of the divorce, because if he said, 'You are divorced: strong, or severe, or long, or wide' and did not mention 'divorce,' it is revocable, because it is not suitable to be an attribute of divorce but is suitable to be an attribute of the woman, as mentioned by al-Asbijabi. And it is conditioned by his saying 'long' or 'wide' because if he said, 'You are divorced: of such length and such width,' then it is one irrevocable, and it does not become three even if he intends it, because length and width indicate strength but they are for a single thing, as if he said, 'one divorce of such length,' so the intention of three is not valid. Thus in Kafi al-Hakim. Hence, some scholars explicitly stated in their commentary that the correct view is that three does not occur with 'long' or 'wide' even if he intended it, and they attributed it to Shams al-A'imma, and they preferred it because intention only works in a probable [word], and 'divorce' with the tā of singularity does not admit three. And it is conditioned by what was mentioned regarding attributes, because if he described it with what it cannot be described, the description is void and it is revocable, like saying 'a divorce that does not fall on you' or 'in which I have option'; and if it can be described but does not indicate an increase in its effect, like saying 'the best divorce, the most proper, the most complete, the last, the most perfect, the most excellent, the fullest,' then it is revocable and she is divorced according to the sunna in its time, and if he intended three, it is three according to the sunna. Thus in Kafi al-Hakim. And al-Asbijabi mentioned that it is revocable in the apparent narration whether the state is menstruation or purity. And he mentioned what al-Hakim established from Abu Yusuf that the conclusion is that description that indicates increase necessitates irrevocability.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage addresses various descriptive phrases in divorce. The first part discusses 'filling the house' (ملء البيت): if a man says 'you are divorced by filling the house,' the divorce is irrevocable because it implies greatness or abundance. If he intends greatness, it is irrevocable; if abundance, also irrevocable; if no intention, fewer is assumed (one). Next, words like 'strong,' 'severe,' 'long,' 'wide' when used to describe the divorce (e.g., 'you are divorced with a strong divorce') make it irrevocable because they indicate a severity that makes the marriage irreparable. However, if the husband uses these words as attributes of the woman without mentioning 'divorce' (e.g., 'you are divorced: strong' without specifying 'divorce'), it is revocable because they can describe the woman. The text then specifies that saying 'long' or 'wide' (طويلة or عريضة) as attributes of the divorce results in one irrevocable divorce, not three, even if he intended three, because these adjectives indicate strength but apply to a single entity. The commentary also covers that describing divorce with qualities that do not indicate increase (like 'best,' 'most proper,' etc.) results in a revocable divorce, and if he intended three it is three but sunna-recommended. Finally, al-Asbijabi and al-Hakim confirm that any description indicating increase leads to irrevocability.

Key Terms
البينونة (al-baynūna): Irrevocability of divorce; the marriage cannot be resumed without a new contract.
ملء البيت (mil' al-bayt): A descriptive phrase meaning 'filling the house', used to indicate the magnitude or quantity of the divorce.

C) One irrevocable divorce

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'filling the house' makes divorce irrevocable, and intention determines whether it is one or multiple.

Strong, Long, Wide: Describing the Divorce vs. the Woman
Rulings on adjectives like strong, severe, long, wide

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between using adjectives like 'strong', 'long', and 'wide' to describe the divorce (bāʾin) versus describing the woman (rajʿī) and apply the ruling on 'long' and 'wide' preventing three divorces.

Core Idea
Adjectives indicating increase cause irrevocability only when attributed to the divorce itself, not the woman.

When a husband says 'You are divorced with a strong divorce' or 'a long divorce,' the divorce becomes irrevocable. However, if he simply says 'You are divorced: strong' without specifying the word 'divorce,' the adjective is considered a description of the woman, leading to a revocable divorce. Additionally, saying 'long' or 'wide' as attributes of the divorce results in one irrevocable divorce, not three, even if intended otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the adjective used
Determine whether the adjective is 'strong', 'severe', 'long', 'wide', etc.
2
Distinguish the referent
Check if the adjective is attached to the word 'divorce' (e.g., 'a strong divorce') or to the woman (e.g., 'You are divorced: strong').
If 'divorce' is mentioned, the adjective describes the divorce. If not, it describes the woman.
3
Apply the ruling for describing the divorce
If the adjective describes the divorce and indicates increase, the divorce is bāʾin (irrevocable).
Examples: 'strong divorce', 'long divorce', 'wide divorce'.
4
Apply the ruling for describing the woman
If the adjective describes the woman, the divorce is rajʿī (revocable) because the description does not affect the divorce's type.
Example: 'You are divorced: strong' (without mentioning 'divorce' as the described noun).
5
Special case: 'long' and 'wide' as descriptions of the divorce
If the husband says 'You are divorced of such length and such width,' it is one bāʾin divorce, not three, because 'length' and 'width' indicate strength but apply to a single entity. Intention of three is ineffective.
The singular 'divorce' does not admit the number three.
