Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Interjecting 'Ya Zaniyah' Between Divorce and Condition
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether 'ya zaniyah' separates conditional divorce and triggers immediate qadhf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether interjecting 'ya zaniyah' between divorce and condition separates the condition and constitutes immediate qadhf, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A call like 'O adulteress' between divorce and condition does not break the speech – both are suspended until the condition is fulfilled.

When a husband utters a conditional divorce and interjects a slanderous address, does the condition remain intact? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on this precise point. This lesson examines the positions of Abu Hanifa (via the apparent narration) and the two Sahibayn, and explains why the relied-upon view treats the interjection as part of the same speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core act
The husband says: 'You are divorced, O adulteress, if you enter the house.' Identify the divorce, the condition, and the interjection.
2
State the three positions
Apparent narration: both divorce and qadhf are suspended. Abu Yusuf: immediate qadhf and immediate divorce. Muhammad: divorce suspended, qadhf immediate.
Each position turns on whether the interjection is a separator.
3
Trace the rationale
The apparent narration treats 'ya zaniyah' as a call for summoning, not a definitive accusation, so it is part of the same speech. Sahibayn see it as a complete sentence that cannot be suspended.
Analogy given: 'You are divorced O 'Amrah if you enter' – the call does not break suspension.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
If the condition is not fulfilled (e.g., she never enters): under apparent narration, no qadhf; under Sahibayn, qadhf occurs immediately, requiring li'an.
This is the practical difference.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) is the relied-upon position for fatwa. The interjection does not separate, so both divorce and qadhf are suspended.
Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., Ibn Abidin) uphold this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن تقدم قوله «يا زانية» على الشرط والجزاء أو على الإيجاب والاستثناء أو تأخر عنهما كان قذفاً في الحال لأن قوله «يا زانية» للاستحضار عرفاً لكونه نداء ولإثبات صفة الزنا وضعاً فكان ملائماً للخطاب من حيث كونه للاستحضار غير ملائم له من حيث إنه إثبات صفة في المنادى فتوفر على الشبهين حظهما فيتعلق إذا كان موسطاً ويتجز إذا كان ظرفاً أو متأخراً عملاً بالشبهين. وعن أبي يوسف أنه لا يكون المتخلل فاصلاً لأنه كلام تام لا يقبل التعليق فلم يتعلق الطلاق فكان قذفاً فيقع الطلاق للحال ويجب اللعان. وعن محمد يتعلق ما يقبل التعليق وهو الطلاق لا القذف ويجب اللعان وجه ظاهر الرواية أن «يا زانية وإن كان جزاء إلا أن المراد منه هنا النفي دون التحقيق أو لأنه نداء والنداء لا يفصل لأنه لإعلام المخاطب بما يراد به فكان من نفس الكلام، ولهذا لو قال أنت طالق يا عمرة إن دخلت الدار تعلق الطلاق وإذا لم يكن فاصلاً تعلق الطلاق بالشرط فيتعلق القذف أيضاً لأنه من نفي الكلام ولأنه أقرب إلى الشرط، وإذا تعلق الأبعد كان الأقرب أولى. فإن قيل : لم يعلق القذف بالشرط بل ناداها فيكون القذف مرسلاً؟ قلنا : لم نعلقه نصاً بل حكماً لكون الكلام واحداً، فإذا ذكر الشرط في الأخير وإذا تعلق يا زانية لم يكن قذفاً في الحال كذا عند وجود الشرط لأن الدخول لا يجعل غير الزاني زانياً . ا هـ ملخصاً
And if his statement 'O adulteress' precedes the condition and the consequence, or precedes the affirmative and the exception, or comes after them, it is immediate slander (qadhf) because his statement 'O adulteress' is conventionally for summoning as it is a call, and by definition it is for affirming the attribute of adultery, so it is fitting for the address in terms of being for summoning, unfitting in terms of affirming an attribute in the one addressed, so each of the two aspects takes its share; thus it becomes attached if it is in the middle, and becomes separate if it occurs before or after, acting on both aspects. From Abu Yusuf: the intervening [word] does not serve as a separation because it is a complete sentence that does not accept suspension, so the divorce does not become suspended, thus it is immediate qadhf, and the divorce occurs immediately, and li'an becomes obligatory. From Muhammad: what accepts suspension becomes suspended, which is the divorce, not the qadhf, and li'an becomes obligatory. The rationale of the apparent narration is that 'O adulteress' even though it is a condition, here the intent is negation not affirmation, or because it is a call, and a call does not separate because it is for informing the addressee of what is intended, so it is part of the speech itself. Hence if he said 'You are divorced O 'Amrah if you enter the house', the divorce becomes suspended. And if it is not a separator, the divorce becomes suspended by the condition, so also the qadhf becomes suspended because it is part of the same speech and because it is closer to the condition. And if the farther is suspended, the nearer is more deserving. If it is said: you did not suspend the qadhf by the condition, rather you called her, so the qadhf is absolute? We say: we did not suspend it explicitly but by ruling because the speech is one. So when he mentions the condition at the end, and if it becomes attached to 'O adulteress', it is not immediate qadhf, thus in the presence of the condition, because entering [the house] does not make a non-adulterer an adulterer. End quote, summarized.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of interjecting the phrase 'ya zaniyah' (O adulteress) between a divorce statement and a condition. The core issue: does calling the wife 'adulteress' constitute immediate qadhf (slander requiring li'an), or does it become suspended along with the divorce? According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), if the phrase 'ya zaniyah' is interjected between the divorce and the condition, it does not separate the speech, so the divorce and the qadhf both become suspended on the condition. However, Abu Yusuf holds that the interjection is a complete sentence that cannot be suspended, so the qadhf is immediate and the divorce also occurs immediately. Muhammad al-Shaybani distinguishes: the divorce can be suspended but the qadhf cannot, so the divorce is suspended while the qadhf is immediate, and li'an becomes obligatory. The commentator explains that in the apparent narration, 'ya zaniyah' is considered a call for summoning, not a definitive accusation, so it is part of the same speech and does not break the continuity. The practical difference: under the apparent narration, if the condition is not fulfilled, there is no qadhf; under Abu Yusuf's view, there is immediate qadhf regardless.

Key Terms
قذف (qadhf): Slanderous accusation of adultery or fornication, requiring li'an (mutual imprecation) if the accuser is the spouse.
لعان (li'an): The mutual imprecation between spouses when the husband accuses the wife of adultery without evidence, dissolving the marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), if a husband says 'You are divorced, O adulteress, if you enter the house', what is the ruling on qadhf?
A) Qadhf occurs immediately
B) Qadhf is suspended along with the divorce
C) Qadhf is negated entirely
D) The husband must perform li'an immediately

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a call interjected between a conditional divorce and its condition does not separate the speech; both divorce and the implied qadhf are suspended until the condition is fulfilled.

intermediate
8 min
Death of Wife Before Completing Divorce Count: The Number is Void
Commentary on ruling that number is void if wife dies before its completion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a wife dies after the divorce formula but before the number is uttered, and identify the conditions for the number to take effect.

Core Idea
The number of divorces takes effect only if completed while the wife is alive; her death renders the number void.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq clarifies a specific scenario in Talaq: what happens if the husband pronounces the divorce formula but the wife dies before he finishes stating the intended number? The commentator explains that the number is void because it was not fully uttered while the wife was alive. This ruling hinges on the principle that the number must be connected (muttasil) and the wife must be alive at the moment the number is spoken. We will unpack the conditions and exceptions.

Guided Path
1
State the Core Ruling
When the wife dies after the divorce formula but before the number is completed, the number is void (laghā). Only the formula itself may take effect as one divorce, if it was properly uttered.
The text says 'nothing occurs' regarding the number, but the initial formula stands.
2
Understand the Underlying Principle
Divorce occurs either with the number (if mentioned) or with the formula alone (if no number is mentioned). The number cannot be implied by necessity when it is not spoken.
This prevents interpreting the number from the context when the wife dies.
3
Examine the Condition of Connection (Ittisāl)
The number must be connected to the formula. A pause for breath does not break the connection, but a deliberate pause without breath, or interruption by another person, does break it. Interruption by a phrase like 'bear witness' (without 'fa') also breaks it.
If the connection is broken, only the formula counts (one divorce).
4
Differentiate Scenarios: Death of Wife vs. Death of Husband
If the husband dies after the formula but before the number, the number is not void; one divorce occurs because the utterance is complete from his side. The restriction to her death is because she was the recipient.
The text contrasts: 'her death' voids the number; 'his death' does not.
5
Apply to Conditional and Multi-Utterance Cases
If the divorce is conditional (e.g., 'if you enter the house'), and she dies before the condition is stated, no divorce occurs because the statement depends on its end. If two formulas are combined with a condition at the end, the whole becomes conditional and void if she dies before the condition is expressed.
This shows the importance of the full utterance in determining the effect.

قوله: (ولو ماتت بعد الإيقاع قبل العدد لغا أي لو ماتت المرأة مدخولة أو غير مدخولة بعد الصيغة قبل تمام العدد لم يقع شيء لما قدمناه أن الواقع عند ذكره به وعند عدمه الوقوع بالصيغة فلا حاجة أن يجعل العدد ثابتاً بطريق الاقتضاء عند عدم ذكره، وقدمنا الدليل على أن الوقوع بالعدد عند قوله أنت طالق واحدة أولاً وقدمنا أن الوقوع بالمصدر والوصف عند ذكرهما أيضاً. ويدخل في العدد أصله وهو الواحد ولا بد من كون العدد متصلاً بالإيقاع ولا يضر الانقطاع لانقطاع النفس فإن قال أنت طالق وسكت من غير انقطاع النفس ثم فواحدة، ولو انقطع النفس أو أخذ إنسان فمه قال ثلاثاً فثلاث. أطلق في الكتاب وهو محمول على ما إذا قال على الفور عند رفع اليد من فمه، ولو قال لغير المدخولة أنت طالق يا فاطمة أو يا زينب ثلاثاً تقع الثلاث، ولو قال أنت طالق اشهدوا ثلاثاً فواحدة، ولو قال فاشهدوا فثلاث كذا في الظهيرية وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو قال لها أنت طالق إن دخلت الدار فماتت قبل قوله إن دخلت لم تطلق لأن صدر الكلام يتوقف على آخره لوجود ما يغيره وهو ذكر الشرط في آخره، فخرج عن أن يكون ايقاعاً وإلى أنه لو قال أنت طالق إن شاء الله فماتت المرأة قبل الاستثناء لم يقع شيء، والمسألتان في المحيط والذخيرة. وفيها : إذا قال لها أنت طالق وأنت طالق فماتت المرأة قبل أن يتكلم بالثاني كانت طالقاً واحدة لأن كل كلام عامل في الوقوع إنما يعمل إذا صادفها وهي حية ولو قال أنت طالق وأنت طالق إن دخلت الدار فماتت المرأة عند الأول أو الثاني لا يقع لأن الكلام المعطوف بعضه على بعض إذا اتصل الشرط بآخره يخرج عن أن يكون إيقاعاً. وفيه: لو قال لها أنت طالق ثلاثاً يا عمرة فماتت قبل قوله يا عمرة طلقت لأنه ليس بمغير ا.هـ. وقيد بموتها احترازاً عن موته لما في الخانية : ولو أراد أن يقول أنت طالق ثلاثاً فلما قال أنت طالق مات أو أخذ إنسان فمه يقع واحدة ا هـ . وفي المعراج : قيد بموتها لأن بموت الزوج قبل ذكر العدد تقع واحدة لأن الزوج وصل لفظ الطلاق بذكر العدد في موتها وذكر العدد حصل بموتها، وفي موت الزوج انصرف إلى جميع الكلام وإذا تعلق يا زانية لم يكن قذفاً في الحال كذا عند وجود الشرط لأن الدخول لا يجعل غير الزاني زانياً . ا هـ ملخصاً
His statement: (And if she dies after the commencement before the number, it is void) meaning if the wife, whether consummated or not, dies after the formula before the completion of the number, nothing occurs because we have previously established that what actually occurs is with its mention, and in its absence, occurrence is with the formula, so there is no need to consider the number as established by way of implication when it is not mentioned. And we have previously presented the proof that occurrence is with the number when he says 'You are divorced once' first, and we have presented that occurrence is with the infinitive and description when they are also mentioned. And the origin of the number, which is one, is included in it, and it is necessary for the number to be connected to the commencement, and interruption due to breath does not harm. So if he says 'You are divorced' and is silent without interruption of breath, then (said) 'once', (it is effective). And if his breath is interrupted or another person took his mouth, then he said 'three', it is three. It is absolute in the book, and it is interpreted as when he says immediately upon removing his hand from his mouth. And if he says to a non-consummated wife 'You are divorced, O Fatima' or 'O Zaynab, three', the three occur. And if he says 'You are divorced, bear witness, three', then one occurs. And if he says 'then bear witness', three occur, as in al-Zahiriyyah. And the author indicated that if he says to her 'You are divorced if you enter the house' and she dies before his saying 'if you enter', she is not divorced because the beginning of the statement depends on its end due to the presence of something that changes it, which is the mention of the condition at its end, so it ceases to be a commencement. And (he indicated) that if he says 'You are divorced, if Allah wills' and the wife dies before the exception, nothing occurs. Both issues are in al-Muhit and al-Dhakhirah. And in it: If he says to her 'You are divorced and you are divorced' and the wife dies before he speaks the second, she is divorced once because each speech is effective in occurrence only when it finds her alive. And if he says 'You are divorced and you are divorced if you enter the house' and the wife dies at the first or second, it does not occur because connected statements when the condition is attached to the last, it ceases to be a commencement. And in it: If he says to her 'You are divorced three times, O Amrah' and she dies before his saying 'O Amrah', she is divorced because it is not a changer. End. And he restricted it to her death to exclude his death, as in al-Khaniyyah: And if he intended to say 'You are divorced three times', then when he said 'You are divorced', he died or another took his mouth, one divorce occurs. End. And in al-Mi'raj: He restricted it to her death because with the death of the husband before mentioning the number, one divorce occurs because the husband connected the word of divorce with mentioning the number at her death, and the mention of the number occurred by her death. And in the husband's death, it refers to the entire speech. And if it relates to 'O adulteress', it is not slander immediately, as in the presence of a condition, because 'entry' does not make a non-adulterer an adulterer. End, summarized.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling from the base text (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) regarding the effect of the wife's death after the divorce formula but before the number is completed. The commentator explains that if the wife dies after the utterance of 'You are divorced' but before the husband states the intended number (e.g., three or one), the number is void (lagha) and no divorce occurs by virtue of the number. The principle is that when the number is mentioned, the divorce occurs with that number; when it is not mentioned, the divorce occurs by the formula alone (the word 'divorced' itself). Therefore, if she dies before the number, there is no need to infer the number by implication. The number must be connected to the utterance; a pause for breath does not break the connection, but a deliberate pause without breath or interruption by another's action does break it. The text then gives specific scenarios: if he says 'You are divorced' and pauses without a breath then adds 'once', it counts as one. If his breath is interrupted or someone covers his mouth and then he says 'three', it counts as three, interpreting the base text as referring to immediate utterance after the interruption. For a non-consummated wife, if he says 'You are divorced, O Fatima, three', the three occur even though the name is inserted; the name does not change the ruling. However, if he says 'You are divorced, bear witness, three', only one divorce occurs because 'bear witness' is a separate phrase that breaks the connection; but if he says 'then bear witness' using fa (then), the connection remains and three occur. The author also addresses conditional divorce: if he says 'You are divorced if you enter the house' and she dies before he says 'if you enter', she is not divorced because the statement depends on its end. Similarly, if he says 'You are divorced, if Allah wills' and she dies before the exception (insha'Allah), nothing occurs. If he says two consecutive divorce formulas ('You are divorced and you are divorced') and she dies before the second, only one divorce occurs because each utterance requires her life at the moment it is spoken. If the two are combined with a condition at the end ('if you enter the house'), the entire statement becomes conditional and ceases to be an immediate commencement, so no divorce occurs if she dies before the condition is expressed. If he says 'You are divorced three times, O Amrah' and she dies before the name, she is divorced because the name is not a changer. The restriction to her death is to distinguish from his death: if he dies before stating the number, one divorce occurs because the wording is complete and the number was intended but not spoken; her death prevents the number because she was not alive to receive it. The passage ends with a comparison to slander using 'O adulteress' where a condition prevents immediate effect.

Key Terms
إيقاع (īqā‘): The act of pronouncing divorce; the initial utterance of the divorce formula.
عدد (‘adad): The number of divorces (e.g., one, two, three) stated after the formula.
لغا (laghā): Void, ineffective; here meaning the number does not take effect.
صيغة (ṣīghah): The verbal formula of divorce, e.g., 'You are divorced'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says 'You are divorced' then says 'if you enter the house' but before completing the condition, the wife dies. What is the ruling?
A) One divorce occurs because the formula was spoken.
B) No divorce occurs because the statement was conditional and she died before the condition was fully expressed.
C) Three divorces occur by default.
D) The divorce is suspended until her resurrection.

Key Takeaway
The number of divorces must be fully uttered while the wife is alive; her death before completion renders the number void, though the initial formula may still effect one divorce if properly spoken.

Rulings on Talaq with Conjunctions (Waw, Fa, Thumma, Bal)
Explains the effect of conjunctive particles on the number of talaqs for non-consummated and consummated wives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that customary usage ('urf) determines whether a conjunctive expression in talaq forms a single number or separate pronouncements, and distinguish the effect for consummated vs. non-consummated wives.

Core Idea
Customary usage determines whether a talaq conjunction forms a single number or separate pronouncements.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq examines how conjunctive particles affect the number of talaqs when a husband uses non-customary number expressions like 'one and one hundred'. The key principle is that language is interpreted according to 'urf, not just grammar. You will see an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad (backed by Abu Hanifa) and Abu Yusuf, and learn how the ruling differs for consummated and non-consummated wives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wife's status
Determine whether the wife is consummated (madkhul biha) or not (ghayr madkhul biha). This changes the outcome because a non-consummated wife becomes fully separated by one talaq, so subsequent talaqs cannot occur.
2
Classify the conjunctive particle
Identify the particle used: waw (و), fa (ف), thumma (ثم), or bal (بل). Each has a different effect on whether the numbers are joined or separate.
Waw is the base; fa implies immediacy, thumma sequence, and bal indicates retraction and addition.
3
Apply the 'urf principle
Check whether the expression is customary. For example, 'one hundred and one' is customary, but 'one and one hundred' is not. For non-customary expressions, the numbers are treated as separate pronouncements, so only the first talaq takes effect (unless the wife is consummated and the first talaq is not retractable).
Customary usage is a zanni dalil; it overrides literal word order.
4
Consider the intra-Hanafi dispute
Note that Abu Yusuf disagrees with Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on expressions like 'one and one hundred'. According to Abu Yusuf, both orders are equivalent and yield three talaqs for a consummated wife. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
The dispute hinges on whether the expression is treated as a single number or separate numbers.
5
Handle special cases: condition and 'bal'
If the husband says 'three if you wish' and she says 'one and one and one', three talaqs occur because the condition is fulfilled at the end of her speech. For 'bal' ('no, rather'), if a consummated wife is told 'one, no rather two', three talaqs occur; for a non-consummated wife, only one occurs.
Bal indicates retraction from the first number and addition, but the first talaq cannot be retracted for a consummated wife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو مختار في التعبير لغة كما قدمناه وقيدنا بتأخير النصف عن الواحدة لأنه لو قدمه عليها بأن قال أنت طالق نصفاً وواحدة وقعت واحدة لأنه غير مستعمل على هذا الوجه فلم يجعل كله كلاماً واحداً وعزاه في المحيط إلى محمد وفيه: لو قال أنت طالق واحدة وعشراً وقعت واحدة بخلاف أحد عشر فإنه يقع الثلاث لعدم العطف، وكذا لو قال واحدة ومائة أو واحدة وألف أو واحدة وعشرين فإنه يقع واحدة لأن هذا غير مستعمل في المعتاد فإنه يقال في العادة مائة وواحدة وألف وواحدة فلم يجعل هذه الجملة كلاماً واحداً بل اعتبر عطفاً. وقال أبو يوسف: تقع الثلاث لأن قوله مائة وواحدة وواحدة ومائة سواء. وقيد بكونه مخاطباً لها بالعدد لأنه لو قال لها أنت طالق ثلاثاً إن شئت فقالت شئت واحدة وواحدة وواحدة طلقت ثلاثاً كما في المعراج وغيره، لأن تمام الشرط بآخر كلامها وما لم يتم الشرط لا يقع الجزاء. وإذا علم الحكم في العطف بالواو علم بالفاء «وثم» بالأولى لاقتضاء الفاء التعقيب و ثم الترتيب. وأما «بل» فإذا قال للمدخولة أنت طالق واحدة لا بل ثنتين تقع الثلاث لأنه أخبر أنه غلط في إيقاع الواحدة ورجع عنها وقصد إيقاع الثنتين قائماً مقام الواحدة فصح إيقاع الثنتين ولم يصح الرجوع عن الواحدة ولو قال ذلك لغير المدخولة تقع واحدة.
It is the chosen expression linguistically as we have previously mentioned, and we restricted it by delaying the half from the one because if he preceded it with it saying "You are divorced half and one," one occurs because it is not customarily used in this manner, so all is not considered one speech. Al-Muhit attributes to Muhammad: In it: If he said "You are divorced one and ten," one occurs, unlike eleven, for it occurs three due to lack of conjunction. Similarly, if he said "one and one hundred" or "one and one thousand" or "one and twenty," one occurs because this is not customary usage; customarily one says "one hundred and one" and "one thousand and one," so this sentence is not considered one speech but rather conjunction is considered. Abu Yusuf said: Three occur because his saying "one hundred and one" and "one and one hundred" are equal. And it is restricted that he addresses her with the number because if he said to her "You are divorced three if you wish," and she said "I wish one and one and one," she is divorced three as in al-Mi'raj and others, because completion of the condition is at the end of her speech, and as long as the condition is not fulfilled, the consequence does not occur. And when the ruling on conjunction with waw is known, it is known with fa and thumma a fortiori due to fa requiring immediacy and thumma sequence. As for bal: If he says to a consummated wife "You are divorced one. No, rather two," three occur because he informs that he made a mistake in effecting one and retracts from it and intends to effect two standing in place of one, so effecting two is valid, but retracting from one is not valid. Were he to say that to a non-consummated wife, one occurs.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the effect of conjunctive particles in talaq expressions. The author discusses the case where a husband says "You are divorced half and one" for a non-consummated wife: only one talaq occurs because the expression is not customary. Similarly, saying "one and ten" results in one talaq, but "eleven" (without waw) results in three because the conjunction is absent. Custom dictates that one says "one hundred and one" not "one and one hundred," so the latter is treated as a conjunction of separate numbers, yielding only one talaq. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that both expressions are equivalent and should give three talaqs. The author then notes that if the condition is expressed as "three if you wish" and the wife responds "one and one and one," three talaqs occur because the condition is fulfilled only at the end of her speech. The ruling for 'fa' and 'thumma' follows from that of 'waw' a fortiori. For 'bal', if a consummated wife is told "one, no rather two," three talaqs occur because the first talaq is not retractable and the second is validly added. For a non-consummated wife, only one occurs because she becomes separated by the first talaq.

Key Terms
عطف (atf): Conjunction of words using particles like waw, fa, thumma.
غير المدخول بها (ghayr al-madkhul biha): A wife who has not been consummated; she becomes fully separated by one talaq.
لا بل (la bal): A phrase meaning 'no, rather' used to correct a previous statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his consummated wife: 'You are divorced one and one hundred.' How many talaqs occur according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) One talaq
B) Three talaqs
C) One hundred and one talaqs
D) No talaq because the expression is unclear

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, customary usage ('urf) is the decisive factor in interpreting ambiguous talaq expressions involving conjunctions, and the majority position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is the mufta bihi.

Talaq with Temporal Words (Qabla, Ba'da, Ma'a) for Non-Consummated Wife
شرح حكم الطلاق بكلمات قبل وبعد ومع لغير المدخول بها

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the number of talaqs that occur when a non-consummated wife is divorced using expressions like 'one before one' or 'one before it one', and distinguish the rulings based on the presence of a pronoun.

Core Idea
In talaq with temporal words, the presence or absence of a pronoun determines which divorce is described, affecting the count for a non-consummated wife.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the nuanced rulings when divorce is expressed using temporal words like 'before', 'after', and 'with'. The key is the syntactic function: if the temporal word carries a pronoun, it describes the second talaq; if not, it describes the first. For a non-consummated wife, this determines whether one or two talaqs occur. Understanding these rules is essential for accurate fatwa in marriage matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the temporal word and its syntax
Determine whether the word 'qabla', 'ba'da', or 'ma'a' is used, and whether it is attached to a pronoun (like 'qablaha' or 'ba'adaha') or not.
If no pronoun, the word describes the first talaq; if with pronoun, it describes the second.
2
Analyze the effect on the first talaq
For a non-consummated wife, the first talaq causes immediate separation. If the temporal word describes the first talaq as occurring before the second, the second cannot occur because the wife becomes separated by the first.
Example: 'one before one' (no pronoun) → only one talaq occurs.
3
Apply the rule for descriptions with a pronoun
If the temporal word has a pronoun (e.g., 'one before it one'), it describes the second talaq. This implies that the second talaq is being described relative to the first, but since both are pronounced in the present, they are treated as simultaneous, resulting in two talaqs.
Example: 'one before it one' → two talaqs occur.
4
Distinguish 'ma'a' (with) expressions
The word 'ma'a' implies conjunction. Regardless of the pronoun, both talaqs occur together. However, Abu Yusuf holds that if a pronoun is used (e.g., 'ma'aha wahida'), only one talaq occurs because the pronoun assigns the 'with' to a specific talaq.
The majority view (mufta bihi) is that two talaqs occur.
5
Compare with the consummated wife case
For a consummated wife, two talaqs occur in all expressions because the first talaq does not cause immediate separation (it is revocable), so the second can take effect even if described as after the first.
This lesson focuses on the non-consummated case; the consummated case is simpler.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتمامه في المحيط من باب عطف الطلاق على الطلاق بكلمة لا بل والمسائل الثلاث هي «قبل و بعد» و «مع» إما قبل فاسم لزمان متقدم على ما أضيفت إليه وأما بعد فاسم لزمان متأخر على ما أضيفت إليه والأصل أن الظرف متى بين اسمين فإن لم يقرن بها الكناية كان صفة للأول تقول «جاءني زيد قبل عمرو» القبلية فيها صفة لزيد. وإن قرن بها الكناية كان صفة للثاني تقول «جاءني زيد قبله عمرو» فإذا قال أنت طالق واحدة قبل واحدة فقد أوقع الأولى قبل الثانية فبانت بها فلا تقع الثانية، ولو قال بعدها واحدة فكذلك لأنه وصف الثانية بالبعدية، ولو لم يصفها به لم تقع فهذا أولى. وأما إذا قال واحدة قبلها واحدة يقع ثنتان لأن إيقاع الطلاق في الماضي إيقاع في الحال لامتناع الاستناد إلى الماضي فيقترنان فتقع ثنتان، وكذا في واحدة بعد واحدة لأنه جعل البعدية صفة للأولى فاقتضى إيقاع الثانية قبلها فكان إيقاعاً في الحال فيقترنان. وهذا كله في غير المدخول بها، وفي المدخول بها تقع ثنتان في الكل واستشكل في واحدة قبل واحدة لأن كون الشيء قبل غيره لا يقتضي وجود ذلك الغير على ما ذكر محمد في الزيادات نحو «فتحرير رقبة من قبل أن يتماسا» [المجادلة: ٣] «لنفد البحر قبل أن تنفد كلمات ربي» [الكهف: ١٠٩] وأجيب بأن هذا اللفظ أشعر بالوقوع وكون الشيء قبل غيره يقتضي وجود ذلك الغير ظاهراً وإن لم يستدعه لا محالة، والعمل بالظاهر واجب ما أمكن. وأما «مع» فللقران فلا فرق فيها بين الإتيان بالضمير أو لا فاقتضى وقوعهما معاً. وعن أبي يوسف أنه لو قال معها واحدة تقع واحدة. وفي المحيط : لو قال لغير المدخولة أنت طالق اليوم وأمس تطلق ثنتين كأنه قال واحدة قبلها واحدة.
And its completion in al-Muhit from the chapter of conjoining divorce with divorce using the word 'la bal', and the three issues are 'before', 'after', and 'with'. As for 'before', it is a noun for a time prior to what it is attached to. As for 'after', it is a noun for a time subsequent to what it is attached to. The principle is that when a particle occurs between two nouns, if it is not connected with a pronoun, it is an attribute of the first; you say 'Zaid came before Amr' where the priority is an attribute of Zaid. If it is connected with a pronoun, it is an attribute of the second; you say 'Zaid came, before him Amr'. So if he says 'You are divorced one before one', he has effected the first before the second, so she becomes separated by it, and the second does not occur. Were he to say 'after it one', likewise, because he describes the second with posteriority, and if he did not describe it thus, it would not occur, so this is more apt. As for when he says 'one before it one', two occur because effecting divorce in the past is effecting in the present due to the impossibility of attributing to the past, so they are concurrent and two occur. Similarly in 'one after it one' because he made posteriority an attribute of the first, which requires effecting the second before it, so it is effecting in the present, so they are concurrent. And all this is for the non-consummated wife. For the consummated wife, two occur in all cases, and there is a difficulty in 'one before one' because something being before another does not necessitate the existence of that other, as Muhammad mentioned in al-Ziyadat like 'freeing a slave before they touch' [58:3] and 'the sea would run out before the words of my Lord' [18:109]. The answer is that this expression indicates occurrence, and something being before another necessitates the existence of that other apparently, even if not absolutely, and acting on the apparent is obligatory as much as possible. As for 'with', it is for conjunction, so there is no difference between bringing the pronoun or not, so it requires their occurrence together. From Abu Yusuf: if he said 'with it one', one occurs. In al-Muhit: if he said to a non-consummated wife 'You are divorced today and yesterday', she is divorced twice, as if he said 'one before it one'.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage explains the rulings when a husband uses the temporal words 'qabla' (before), 'ba'da' (after), and 'ma'a' (with) in a talaq expression for a non-consummated wife. The principle is that if the word 'before' or 'after' is attached to the second talaq without a pronoun, it describes the first talaq; if with a pronoun, it describes the second. For example, 'You are divorced one before one' means the first occurs before the second, so the wife becomes separated by the first and the second does not occur. But 'one before it one' means two talaqs occur simultaneously because the husband intends both as past, which is treated as present. The same applies for 'after'. For a consummated wife, two talaqs always occur, though there is a difficulty regarding 'one before one' because the Quranic usage of 'before' does not always imply the existence of the other. The answer is that the expression indicates occurrence in the context of talaq. For 'with', both happen together. Abu Yusuf holds that 'with it one' gives only one talaq.

Key Terms
قبل (qabla): A noun meaning 'before', indicating temporal priority.
بعد (ba'da): A noun meaning 'after', indicating temporal posteriority.
مع (ma'a): A noun meaning 'with', indicating simultaneity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his non-consummated wife: 'You are divorced one before one.' How many talaqs occur according to the Hanafi school?
A) One talaq
B) Two talaqs
C) Three talaqs
D) No talaq because it is impossible

Key Takeaway
For a non-consummated wife, the number of talaqs depends on the syntax of temporal words: 'one before one' yields one talaq, while 'one before it one' yields two; for 'ma'a', the default is two except according to Abu Yusuf.

Resolving the Puzzle of Talaq Attached to Months
Analyzes a poetic question about talaq attached to months and lists eight possible interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of ilgha' al-mukarrar to determine the intended month in a conditional talaq and evaluate the intra-madhhab dispute over the correct parsing method.

Core Idea
Cancel one repeated temporal word; if 'before' remains, month is Shawwal; if 'after', month is Sha'ban.

A husband's ambiguous phrase 'before what after before him Ramadan' leads to eight possible interpretations. The Hanafi school provides a systematic rule to resolve the ambiguity: cancel one of the repeated terms and read the remaining one. This lesson unpacks the rule, applies it to all eight forms, and examines a scholarly dispute over the method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of the phrase
Break down the phrase 'qabla ma ba'da qablihi' into its temporal components: 'before', 'after', and 'before' (repeated). Note the attached pronoun 'hi' (him) referring to Ramadan.
The phrase is a chain of two 'before's and one 'after' in varying orders across the eight forms.
2
State the rule: ilgha' al-mukarrar
Cancel one of the two identical repeated terms (e.g., two 'before's or two 'after's). The remaining word (the non-cancelled repeated term) determines the month: if it is 'before', the month is Shawwal; if 'after', the month is Sha'ban.
This rule is attributed to al-Fath and followed by later Hanafi works.
3
Apply the rule to the eight faces
For each of the eight possible readings, cancel one repeated 'before' or 'after' and identify the remaining word. Match it to the month: e.g., face 2 ('before what after before him') -> cancel one 'before', remaining 'before' -> Shawwal; face 6 ('after what before after him') -> cancel one 'after', remaining 'after' -> Sha'ban.
The passage lists which faces fall into Shawwal (2,3,4) and which into Sha'ban (6,7,8).
4
Distinguish the author's method from the criticized one
The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq criticizes a previous scholar who canceled the first two terms (whether 'before' or 'after') and considered only the last term attached to the pronoun. The author insists that one of the repeated terms must be canceled, not necessarily the first two.
The correct method (following al-Maqdisi) preserves the principle of canceling one repeated item regardless of its position.
5
Conclude the practical outcome
For any valid parsing, the talaq takes effect in the month determined by the rule (Shawwal or Sha'ban). The dispute only concerns the method, not the validity of the talaq.
The mufta bihi is the author's method.

And in Sharh al-Niqaya for al-Shamni: Then of the issues of 'before' and 'after', what was said in verse: 'What says the faqih, may God support him and always give him beneficence, concerning a youth who attached divorce to a month before what after before him Ramadan.' This line can be recited in eight ways: First: 'before what before before him'; second: 'before what after before him'; third: 'before what before after him'; fourth: 'after what before before him'; fifth: 'after what after after him'; sixth: 'after what before after him'; seventh: 'after what after before him'; eighth: 'before what after after him'. The rule regarding what combines 'before' and 'after' is such as mentioned in al-Fath and followed in Sharh Nazm al-Kanz, al-Nahr, and al-Durr al-Mukhtar. Its summary: cancel one of the two repeated with the non-repeated, and consider one of the repeated ones the other wherever it appears first, middle, or last. If its wording is 'before', the intended is Shawwal; if 'after', then Sha'ban. Al-Maqdisi said in his verse commentary: 'Match the before with that which is after, and upon it build the exposition. Ponder with intelligence and acumen, for thereby one grasps the eight faces.' So in the second, third, and fourth faces, it occurs in Shawwal, and in the sixth, seventh, and eighth in Sha'ban. When it becomes clear to you what we have established, you know the incorrectness of what the author mentions of the summary, where he made the canceled ones the first two whether they are 'before' or 'after' or different, and made the considered one the last attached to the pronoun. He mentioned the eight forms branching from the final hemistich and drew at each form the intended month according to what I first established, contrary to what the author mentioned. Then he said in verse: 'Take from me the answer to what was said in verse, a question surrounded by precision, about a youth who attached divorce to a month before what before before him Ramadan.'

Commentary
This passage addresses a complex linguistic puzzle in talaq law: a husband says 'You are divorced in the month before what after before him Ramadan' (or similar). The phrase can be parsed in eight ways depending on how 'before' and 'after' are ordered. The rule to determine the intended month is: cancel one of the repeated terms (e.g., two 'before's) and consider the other. If the remaining word is 'before', the month is Shawwal; if 'after', it is Sha'ban. The author criticizes another scholar's summary, arguing that he incorrectly cancels the first two terms and only considers the last attached to a pronoun. The correct method is to cancel one of the repeated terms and consider the other wherever it appears. The passage then notes that this issue was debated by Ibn al-Hajib and al-Subki, and a later scholar, Ibn al-Ghazzi, compiled the eight forms with the correct months.

Key Terms
قبل ما بعد قبله (qabla ma ba'da qablihi): A phrase meaning 'before what after before him' - a temporal chain used to determine the month of talaq.
إلغاء المتكرر (ilgha' al-mukarrar): Cancellation of one of the repeated words in a temporal chain to determine the intended month.
شوال (Shawwal): The month after Ramadan; if the parsing yields 'before', the intended month is Shawwal.

Determining the Month of Divorce When 'Before' and 'After' Overlap with 'Ma'
Analysis of divorce statements with multiple temporal prepositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the role of 'ما' in a divorce statement with multiple temporal prepositions and determine the effective month of divorce according to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
The same phrase can mean different months depending on whether 'ma' is extra or a relative pronoun.

This lesson examines a subtle linguistic issue in divorce pronouncements. When a husband uses phrases like 'before what after it is Ramadan,' the word 'ما' can have different grammatical functions, leading to different rulings. Understanding this distinction is crucial for accurately determining when a divorce takes effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the function of 'ma'
Determine if 'ما' is extra (za'ida), relative pronoun (mawsula), or described noun (mawsoofa). This classification changes the meaning.
The text explicitly states: 'ما إما أن تكون زائدة أو موصولة أو موصوفة'.
2
Apply the cancellation rule for 'qabl' and 'ba'd'
When multiple prepositions appear, consecutive 'قبل' and 'بعد' cancel each other in a specified order. For example, 'قبل بعد' becomes 'قبل'.
This is derived from the principle: 'كل شهر بعد قبله وقبل بعده'.
3
Trace the month sequence
Using the reference month (e.g., Ramadan), work backwards or forwards through the months to find which month is being referred to after cancellation.
For instance, if the reference is Ramadan, 'before it' is Sha'ban, 'after it' is Shawwal.
4
Derive the ruling
Based on the combination, state the month in which the divorce occurs. There are eight possible forms; the ruling depends on whether 'ما' is extra or not.
The passage provides detailed outcomes for each form.

The before and after: that before and after are cancelled because every month is after its before and before its after. So what remains is 'before it is Ramadan' which means Shawwal, or 'after it is Ramadan' which means Sha'ban. End summary. In the first of these four, if 'ma' is cancelled, it occurs in Shawwal as if he said 'before before its after is Ramadan', then 'before' is cancelled by 'after' so it becomes as if he said 'before it is Ramadan' and that is Shawwal. And in pure after it occurs in Jumada al-Akhirah because after it is Rajab, and after this after is Sha'ban, and after after after is Ramadan.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of rulings regarding divorce statements that include multiple temporal prepositions 'before' (قبل) and 'after' (بعد) relative to a reference month like Ramadan. The discussion revolves around the effect of the word 'ما' and how it changes the outcome. The author first presents a scenario where 'ما' is considered extra (زائدة) versus being a relative pronoun (موصولة) or described noun (موصوفة). When 'ما' is extra, the prepositional phrases cancel each other according to specified rules, e.g., 'before what after it is Ramadan' reduces to 'before it is Ramadan' and the divorce occurs in Shawwal. When 'ما' is not extra, it refers to a specific month, and the divorce occurs differently. The text systematically goes through eight possible combinations (four with 'ما' as extra and four without) and their outcomes. It also addresses the reasoning behind the Hanafi ruling, noting that the issue is linguistic. The final part explains an example: 'a month before what after it is Ramadan'—analyzing the grammatical structure to show that 'Ramadan' is the subject of a clause describing 'month', and the ruling that it falls in Dhul-Hijjah is derived from the sequence of months. The author expresses curiosity why earlier scholars only explained cases of cancellation, but confirms the Hanafi position aligns with this linguistic analysis.

Key Terms
قبل (qabl): Temporal preposition meaning 'before'. In divorce expressions, it specifies a time relative to another event or month.
بعد (ba'd): Temporal preposition meaning 'after'. Similar to 'qabl' but indicates a later time.
ما (ma): A particle that can be extra (زائدة), a relative pronoun (موصولة), or a described noun (موصوفة). Its classification determines the meaning of the temporal phrase.

Interpreting 'Kull' and Temporal Prepositions in Divorce
Ruling on linguistic interpretation of divorce declarations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi rules to determine the number of divorces from declarations containing 'kull' and the prepositions 'before', 'after', and 'with', and identify intra-Hanafi differences in certain acknowledgment scenarios.

Core Idea
The word 'kull' and temporal prepositions can make a divorce declaration multiple or singular depending on context, definite articles, and pronouns.

Divorce declarations can be phrased in complex ways, especially when using words like 'every' and temporal prepositions. The Hanafi school, drawing from works like Talkhis al-Jami', developed precise linguistic rules to interpret such declarations consistently. This lesson unpacks these rules, showing how context determines the number of divorces that occur.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key linguistic elements
Look for the presence of 'kull' (every), the prepositions 'before' (qabl), 'after' (ba'd), and 'with' (ma'), and any pronouns like 'ha'. Note whether the definite article is used with 'kull'.
For example, 'طالق كل تطليقة' vs 'طالق تطليقة مع كل تطليقة'.
2
Apply the 'kull' rule
If 'kull' is used without the definite article, it generalizes; with the definite article, it specifies. For 'kull' coupled with a singular, the default is multiplicity unless the speaker intends singular.
The passage contrasts 'كل تطليقة ثلاث' with the definite article.
3
Interpret temporal prepositions
For 'after' and 'before', the presence of the pronoun 'ha' affects whether the description applies to the later or earlier element. 'With' generally joins the singular to the plural, implying multiple divorces unless intention to the contrary.
'قبلها كل تطليقة' vs 'قبل كل تطليقة' have different effects.
4
Distinguish immediate vs suspended effect
If the declaration includes a future time (like 'after the day of Adha'), it is suspended. 'Before' and 'with' are usually effective immediately unless a condition is attached.
The passage gives examples from al-Tatimma with conditional phrases.
5
Handle acknowledgment cases
For acknowledgments of debt using similar phrasing, the default is two dirhams (or double) except in 'before every dirham' without pronoun, where one dirham is binding. Note the intra-Hanafi difference in the example of 'dirham with every dirham'.
Abu Hanifa: twenty dirhams; the two: six dirhams.

ومن مسائل الظروف الثلاثة ما في تلخيص الجامع من كتاب الطلاق باب الطلاق في الوقت طالق كل تطليقة ثلاث خلاف المعرف إذ عم أجزاءه وأفرد المنكر شبه كل دار وكل الدار كذا طالق تطليقة مع كل ،تطليقة وعكسها لقران المفرد الكل إلا أن ينوي المفرد فيدين للتخصيص كذا بعد كل تطليقة وقبلها كل تطليقة لسبق الكل الفرد إذ هما بالهاء وصف اللاحق ودونه وصف السابق لهذا كان فرداً قبل الدخول في عكس الهاء للعكس، وتعلق في طالق بعد يوم الأضحى وتنجز في قبل وقبلها ومعها إذ إضافة الوقت قلب المشروع المقدور فلغت وبقي الذات بلا قيد كطالق طلاقاً لا يقع إلا غداً أو بالدخول بخلاف بائناً إذ غير محمد يلحق الوصف ولو أقر بمال هكذا لزم فرد في الأولى مثنى في الباقي لجهل الزائد، واعتبر بآخر كل شهر إلا في قبل للصدق بالفرد وعشرون في علي درهم مع كل درهم من الدراهم عنده وستة عندهما. وأصله تعريف الجمع واحد عشر في ضم المشار وأربعة عندهما لامتناع التعدد في المشار حتى لم يتعدد عليها في أنت طالق زوجة ا هـ . وحاصله أنه في الإقرار يلزمه در همان في جميع الصور أعني مع وقبل وبعد إلا في قوله لك علي درهم قبل كل در هم بلا ضمير فإنه يلزمه درهم واحد. فما في التحرير لابن الهمام أنه في الإقرار يلزمه إلمالان مطلقاً ليس بصحيح في الكل. وصرح في الخانية من الإقرار بأنه يلزمه واحد في قوله له علي درهم قبل درهم وأطلق المصنف رحمه الله في مسائل الظروف الثلاث فشمل ما إذا كان الطلاق منجزاً أو معلقاً، ولذا قال في التتمة: إذا قال لامرأته ولم يدخل بها أنت طالق واحدة بعدها واحدة إن دخلت الدار بانت بالأولى ولم يلزمها اليمين لأن هذا منقطع، ولو قال أنت طالق واحدة قبل واحدة إن دخلت الدار لم تطلق حتى تدخل الدار. فإذا دخلت طلقت واحدة ولو قال لها أنت طالق واحدة قبلها واحدة أو معها واحدة أو مع واحدة إن دخلت الدار لم تطلق حتى تدخل الدار فإذا دخلت الدار يقع عليها ثنتان وكذلك الجواب فيما إذا قال أنت طالق واحدة وبعدها أخرى إن دخلت الدار اهـ .
And among the issues of three temporal prepositions is what is in Talkhis al-Jami' from the Book of Divorce, Chapter of Divorce in Time: 'You are divorced each divorce three' contrary to the definite article since it generalizes its parts and makes indefinite singular like 'every house' and 'the house' similarly. 'You are divorced one divorce with every divorce' and its opposite due to joining the singular with the plural except if he intends singular, then he is believed in intention for specification. Similarly, 'after every divorce' and 'before it every divorce' due to the precedence of the plural over the singular, since both with the pronoun 'ha' describe the later, and without it describe the earlier. Hence it was singular before consummation in the opposite of 'ha' for the opposite. And it is suspended in 'divorced after the day of Adha' and effective in 'before' and 'before it' and 'with it' since adding time alters the legislated possible, so it is cancelled and the essence remains without restriction, like 'you are divorced a divorce that does not occur except tomorrow' or 'upon entry', contrary to 'irrevocably' since other than Muhammad attaches the description. And if he acknowledges a debt like this, one is binding in the first, double in the rest due to ignorance of the extra. And consider by the end of every month except in 'before' due to truth of singular. And twenty in 'upon Ali a dirham with every dirham of the dirhams' according to him, and six according to them both. And its origin is definition of the plural: one-eleventh in the inclusion of the partner, and four according to them both due to impossibility of multiplicity in the partner, so that it does not become multiple in 'you are divorced a wife'. End. Its summary: in acknowledgment, two dirhams are binding in all forms, i.e., with, before, and after, except in his saying 'upon you a dirham before every dirham' without pronoun, then one dirham is binding. So what is in al-Tahrir of Ibn al-Humam that in acknowledgment two mals are binding absolutely is not correct in all. And it is stated in al-Khaniyah from acknowledgment that one is binding in his saying 'upon him a dirham before a dirham'. And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) generalized in the issues of three prepositions, including whether the divorce is immediate or suspended. Hence he said in al-Tatimma: If he said to his wife before consummation 'you are divorced one after one if you enter the house', she becomes irrevocably divorced by the first and no oath is required because this is discontinuous. And if he said 'you are divorced one before one if you enter the house', she is not divorced until she enters the house. Then when she enters, one divorce occurs. And if he said 'you are divorced one before it one, or with it one, or with one if you enter the house', she is not divorced until she enters the house, then when she enters two divorces occur. And similarly the answer if he said 'you are divorced one and after it another if you enter the house'.
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings from Talkhis al-Jami' concerning divorce statements that involve multiple time references and the word 'كل' (every). The first example discusses the phrase 'طالق كل تطليقة ثلاث' (you are divorced each divorce three), which contrasts with using the definite article. The general principle is that 'كل' can generalize or specify depending on context. The text then deals with combinations like 'before every divorce' and 'after every divorce', noting that the pronoun 'ها' can affect whether the description applies to the later or earlier element. It also covers suspended divorces linked to future events like 'after the day of Adha' versus immediate effect with 'before'. There is a discussion of acknowledgments (إقرار) using similar temporal phrases, where the number of dirhams owed varies: in most cases two dirhams are due, but if he says 'a dirham before every dirham' without a pronoun, only one is due. The text then cites al-Tatimma with examples of divorce before consummation: if he says 'one after one' suspended on entry, only the first takes effect immediately (making it final since before consummation), but if he says 'one before one', it is all suspended. And if he says 'one before it one' or 'with it one', two divorces occur upon entry. The author emphasizes that these rules apply whether the divorce is immediate or suspended.

Key Terms
كل (kull): A word meaning 'every' or 'all'. In divorce statements, it can imply multiplicity or totality depending on context and presence of defining articles.
ظروف ثلاثية (zuruf thalathiyyah): Three temporal prepositions (قبل، بعد، مع) used in divorce and other declarations to create complex conditional sequences.

D) None

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi jurists developed precise linguistic rules for interpreting divorce declarations involving 'kull' and temporal prepositions, where context and pronouns determine the number of divorces.

Rules of Three Time Words in Talaq: Eight Configurations and Intra-Hanafi Differences
Explaining the eight possible configurations of three time words in divorce statements and their application to conditional talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules of 'before', 'after', and 'with' to determine the number and timing of divorces in both unconditional and conditional divorce statements.

Core Idea
The grammatical role of time words and pronouns determines whether a second divorce is immediate, conditional, or void.

This lesson from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a detailed taxonomy of how the three time words interact in talaq statements, drawing on analogies from acknowledgment law and applying them to suspended divorces. Mastering these rules is crucial for any Hanafi scholar because misinterpreting the wording can lead to an incorrect number of divorces, affecting the marital status and halal relationship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three time words and their order
Locate 'qabl', 'ba'd', and 'ma'a' in the talaq statement. Note their sequence and whether they refer to each other or to the whole.
The eight possible configurations are derived from the order of these words.
2
Determine if the statement includes a pronoun
Check for attached pronouns like 'ha' (meaning 'it'). A pronoun changes the reference: with a pronoun, the description attaches to the later; without, it attaches to the earlier.
This affects whether the number of divorces is single or multiple.
3
Apply the rule for 'with' and 'after'
Generally, 'ma'a' (with) and 'ba'd' (after) imply multiple divorces unless a pronoun is absent, which may make the statement refer to a single individual.
Abu Hanifa holds that 'with' and 'after' connect the whole, resulting in multiple.
4
Apply the rule for 'before'
'Qabl' (before) typically implies a single divorce because it suggests precedence and suspension until the condition is fulfilled.
In conditional talaq, 'before' makes both parts conditional, but only one becomes effective.
5
Analogize from acknowledgment rules
The same principles apply to property acknowledgment: e.g., 'dirham with every dirham' implies multiple dirhams. This analogy confirms the talaq ruling.
Intra-Hanafi differences exist in acknowledgment, which are noted but not always followed in talaq.

زوجة ا هـ . وحاصله أنه في الإقرار يلزمه درهمان في جميع الصور أعني مع وقبل وبعد إلا في قوله لك علي درهم قبل كل درهم بلا ضمير فإنه يلزمه درهم واحد. فما في التحرير لابن الهمام أنه في الإقرار يلزمه قال في التتمة: إذا قال لامرأته ولم يدخل بها أنت طالق واحدة بعدها واحدة إن دخلت الدار بانت بالأولى ولم يلزمها اليمين لأن هذا منقطع، ولو قال أنت طالق واحدة قبل واحدة إن دخلت الدار لم تطلق حتى تدخل And if he said for her 'You are divorced one before it one or with it one or with one if you enter the house', she is not divorced until she enters the house; so when she enters the house, two fall upon her. And similarly the answer in what if he said 'You are divorced one and after it another if you enter the house'. End. With him, with every; the before Ramadan and in pure after falls in Jumada al-Akhira because after it is Rajab and after this after is Sha'ban and after after the after is Ramadan.
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses the intricate rules of divorce statements involving three time words: 'qabl' (before), 'ba'd' (after), and 'ma'a' (with). The author first enumerates eight possible configurations of these words relative to each other, using the example of divorce pronouncements in specific months to illustrate how the arrangement affects the number of divorces that occur. He then draws an analogy from the rules of acknowledgment (iqrar) of property to clarify the underlying principle: in many cases, using 'with' or 'after' implies multiple obligations, while 'before' may imply a single one, depending on whether the statement includes a pronoun. The passage includes intra-Hanafi differences: for example, Abu Hanifa holds that in a statement like 'you are divorced one with every divorce' the whole is connected resulting in multiple divorces, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ in some cases, such as the counting of dirhams in an acknowledgment. The author then applies these rules to conditional divorce before consummation, citing al-Tatimmah: if a man tells his wife 'you are divorced one after one if you enter the house', she is divorced immediately by the first and the condition is void for the second; but if he says 'one before one', she is not divorced until entry, and only one falls. If he says 'one before it one' or 'one with it one', two divorces fall upon entry. These details highlight the importance of wording in talaq and the Hanafi approach to resolving ambiguities through linguistic analysis and qiyas.

Key Terms
الظروف الثلاثة (al-zuruf al-thalatha): The three time words: qabl (before), ba'd (after), and ma'a (with) used in divorce statements.
قبل (qabl): Meaning 'before' in temporal context; used to indicate that one event precedes another.
بعد (ba'd): Meaning 'after'; indicates a subsequent event in time.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Meaning of 'Wa' in Conditional Divorce
Analyzing the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the two companions on the meaning of 'wa' in conditional divorce statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa holds that only one divorce occurs and the Sahibayn hold that two occur, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
In conditional divorce, the conjunctive 'wa' can imply sequence (Abu Hanifa) or simultaneity (Sahibayn), changing the number of divorces.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute: when a husband says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced one and one', how many divorces occur upon entry? The answer hinges on the interpretation of the word 'wa' (and). Abu Hanifa argues that the second divorce is dependent on the first and thus void; the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that both are equally conditioned and occur together. Understanding this disagreement illuminates the Hanafi approach to linguistic analysis in talaq.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the disagreement
The passage focuses on the statement: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced one and one.' Identify the conflicting rulings: Abu Hanifa says one divorce; the Sahibayn say two.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's position and his reasoning
Abu Hanifa interprets 'wa' as sequence (tartib). The second divorce is suspended by means of the first, so when the condition occurs, only the first takes effect; the second becomes void.
He compares to immediate pronouncement: if pronounced immediately, the second cannot occur after the first.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's position and their reasoning
The Sahibayn interpret 'wa' as simultaneity (mu'iyya). Both divorces are equally suspended by the condition and descend together when the condition is fulfilled.
They liken it to multiple conditions: all suspended matters descend together at the condition.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Sahibayn
The text from Fath al-Qadir denies that the first suspension is a cause for the second; the cause is the 'wa' itself. Also, even if the first precedes in suspension, it does not mean its occurrence prevents the second.
The analogy to consecutive oaths is used to show that each can be independent.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa, one divorce occurs, preserving two remaining divorces. Under the Sahibayn, two divorces occur, leaving only one or ending the marriage if it was a triple.
Fath al-Qadir leans towards the Sahibayn's view as more preponderant (arjah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأنت طالق واحدة وواحدة فدخلت يقع واحدة وإن أخر الشرط فثنتان . موجب قوله : (إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق واحدة وواحدة فدخلت يقع واحدة وإن أخر الشرط فثنتان) بأن قال أنت طالق واحدة وواحدة إن دخلت الدار، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : يقع ثنتان فيهما ونسب لأبي حنيفة القول بأن الواو للترتيب أخذاً من قوله بوقوع الواحدة فيما إذا قدم الشرط لأنها لو كانت للجمع لتعلق الكل وليس بصحيح، بل إنما قال بالواحدة لأن هذا عنده تعلق المتأخر بواسطة المتقدم فينزلن كذلك فيسبق الأول فتبطل الكلام محليتها وتوضيحه أن الأول تعلق قبل الثاني لعدم ما يوجب توقفه وتعلق الثاني بواسطته، والثالث بواسطتهما فينزل على الوجه الذي وقع عليه التعليق بخلاف ما إذا كرر الشرط لأن تعلق الثاني بغير شرط الأول ليس بواسطة الأول لأن كلاً جملة مستقلة فتعلق بالشرط الواحد طلقات ليس شيء منها بواسطة شيء فينزلن جميعاً عند الشرط بخلاف ما إذا أخر الشرط لأن تأخره موجب لتوقف الأول لأنه مغير فتعلق الكل به دفعة فينزل دفعة. ونسب إليهما القول بأنها للمعية أخذاً من قولهما بوقوع الثنتين وليس بصحيح، بل قالا بعدما اشتركت في التعلق بواسطة أن تنزل دفعة لأن نزول كل حكم الشرط فتقترن أحكامه كما في تعدد الشرط. قال في فتح القدير، قولهما أرجح وقول الإمام تعلق الثاني بواسطة تعلق الأول؛ إن أريد أنه علة فممنوع بل علته جمع الواو إياه أي الشرط، وإن أريد كونه سابق التعلق سلمناه ولا يفيد كالأيمان المتعاقبة، ولو سلم أن تعلق الأول علة لتعلق الثاني لم يلزم كون نزوله علة لنزوله إذ لا تلازم فجاز كونه علة لتعلقه فيتقدم في التعلق وليس نزوله علة لنزوله بل إذا تعلق الثاني بأي سبب كأن صار الأول متعلقين بشرط وعند نزول الشرط ينزل المشروط مع اهـ. وهذا كله تقرير الأصول وأما تقرير الفروع فوجه قول الإمام أن المعلق بالشرط كالمنجز عند وجوده ولو نجزه حقيقة لم يقع الثانية بخلاف ما إذا أخر الشرط لوجود المغير . كذا ذكر الشارح وحاصل ما في الهداية أن الواو لمطلق الجمع لا تصدق إلا في ضمن معية أو ترتيب، فعلى اعتبار المعية يقع الكل، وعلى اعتبار الترتيب لا يقع إلا واحدة فلا يقع الزائد بالشك وهو أقرب ماوجه به قول الإمام قيد بالواو لأنه لو عطف بالفاء وقدم الشرط وقعت واحدة اتفاقاً على الأصح للتعقيب ولو عطف بـ«ثم وأخر الشرط وقعت واحدة منجزة ولغا ما بعدها، وإن كانت مدخولاً بها تعلق الأخير وتنجز ما قبله وإن تقدم الشرط تعلق الأول وتنجز الثاني فيقع المعلق عند الشرط بعد التزوج الثاني ولغا الثالث وفي المدخول بها تعلق الأول ونجز ما بعده، وعندهما تعلق الكل بالشرط قدمه أو أخره إلا عند وجود الشرط تطلق تعلقه من شرح الفارسي قوله : (كالإيمان) (المتعاقبة قال الرملي : تفسيره لو قال إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق ثم بعد زمان قال إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق فدخلت يقع الكل اتفاقاً. قوله: (ولو عطف يتم وأخر الشرط الخ) قال الرملي : هذا غلط بلا شبهة ولا صحة لهذا
So, 'You are divorced one and one, and she enters, one occurs, and if he delays the condition, two occur.' The cause is his saying: 'If you enter the house, then you are divorced one and one, and she enters, one occurs, and if he delays the condition, two occur' by him saying 'You are divorced one and one if you enter the house.' This is according to Abu Hanifa. And they (the two companions) said: 'Two occur in both cases.' And it is attributed to Abu Hanifa the view that the 'wa' is for sequence, taken from his saying that one occurs when the condition is advanced, because if it were for conjunction, the whole would be suspended, but that is not correct. Rather, he only said one because in his view, the later is suspended by means of the earlier, so they descend accordingly; thus the first precedes and the speech becomes void in its locality. The clarification is that the first is suspended before the second due to the absence of something necessitating its suspension, and the second is suspended by its means, and the third by means of both, so it descends in the manner the suspension occurred. Unlike if the condition is repeated, because the suspension of the second without the condition of the first is not by means of the first; rather, each is an independent sentence, so divorces are suspended by the single condition, none of them by means of another, so they all descend at the condition. Unlike if he delayed the condition, because its delay necessitates the suspension of the first since it is a changer, so all are suspended by it at once and descend at once. And it is attributed to them the view that 'wa' is for simultaneity, taken from their saying that two occur, but that is not correct. Rather, they said that after they share in suspension by means, they descend at once because the descent of each is by the condition, so their rulings coincide as in multiple conditions. He said in Fath al-Qadir: Their view is more preponderant. As for the Imam's view that the second is suspended by means of the suspension of the first: if it is meant that it is a cause, it is denied; rather, its cause is the gathering of the 'wa' to it, i.e., the condition. And if it is meant that it precedes in suspension, we concede that, but it does not benefit, like consecutive oaths. And if it is conceded that the suspension of the first is a cause for the suspension of the second, it does not necessitate that its occurrence is a cause for its occurrence, since there is no necessary correlation. So it is possible that it is a cause for its suspension, thus it precedes in suspension, but its occurrence is not a cause for its occurrence. Rather, if the second is suspended by any cause, it becomes as if the first is two [things] suspended by a condition, and at the occurrence of the condition, the conditioned descends together. End. All this is the establishment of principles. As for the establishment of branches, the face of the Imam's view is that the suspended by condition is like the immediate when it exists; if he were to actually make it immediate, the second would not occur, unlike if he delayed the condition due to the existence of a changer. Thus mentioned the commentator. The summary of what is in al-Hidayah is that 'wa' is for absolute conjunction but is only truthful within simultaneity or sequence. So considering simultaneity, all occur; considering sequence, only one occurs, so the extra does not occur due to doubt. And that is the closest way to support the Imam's view, restricted to 'wa', because if he connected with 'fa' and advanced the condition, one occurs by agreement upon the soundest view for following. And if he connected with 'thumma' and delayed the condition, one occurs immediately and what is after it is void. And if she is one with whom consummation has occurred, the last is suspended and what is before it is immediate; and if the condition is advanced, the first is suspended and the second is immediate, so the suspended occurs at the condition after a second marriage and the third is void. And in the case of consummation, the first is suspended and what follows it is immediate. And according to them, all are suspended by the condition, whether he advanced it or delayed it, except that at the occurrence of the condition she is divorced. Its suspension from the commentary of al-Farsi: His saying 'like consecutive oaths'. Al-Ramli said: Its explanation is that if he said 'If you enter the house, you are divorced', then after a time he said 'If you enter the house, you are divorced', then she enters, all occur by agreement. His saying: 'And if he connects with 'thumma' and delays the condition, etc.' Al-Ramli said: This is a clear error and has no validity.
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of the conjunctive 'wa' (and) in conditional divorce statements. The core issue: if a man says to his wife before consummation, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced one and one', and she enters, how many divorces occur? According to Abu Hanifa, if the condition is stated first (e.g., 'If you enter, you are divorced one and one'), only one divorce occurs because the second 'one' is suspended by means of the first; the first divorce occurs, and the second is void. However, if the condition is stated after the divorces (e.g., 'You are divorced one and one if you enter'), then two divorces occur because the delayed condition makes the entire utterance conditional and both descend together. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that in both scenarios, two divorces occur, because the 'wa' indicates simultaneity (al-mu'iyya) and both divorces are suspended by the single condition. The author explains the reasoning: Abu Hanifa views 'wa' as implying sequence (al-tartib) in this context, so the first divorce takes effect immediately upon the condition, and the second, being dependent on the first, cannot stand. The two companions argue that the 'wa' is for absolute conjunction, and since both divorces are equally conditioned, they occur together. The commentary from Fath al-Qadir prefers the view of the two companions as more preponderant (arjah), but also explains Abu Hanifa's rationale: the suspended (mu'allaq) becomes like the immediate upon fulfillment, so if he had actually executed the divorces immediately, the second would not occur after the first; similarly here. The passage also distinguishes between 'wa', 'fa', and 'thumma' in conditional divorce, showing different rulings. This dispute highlights the importance of linguistic precision in talaq and the Hanafi method of resolving disagreements through a combination of grammatical analysis and legal reasoning.

Key Terms
الواو (al-waw): The Arabic conjunction 'and' (wa). In this context, its interpretation (sequence vs. simultaneity) determines the number of divorces.
التعليق بالشرط (al-ta'liq bi al-shart): Suspending a divorce upon the occurrence of a condition; the divorce is not effective until the condition is fulfilled.
المعية (al-mu'iyya): Simultaneity; the interpretation that the conjunctive 'wa' implies both events occur together.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the statement 'If you enter the house, you are divorced one and one', how many divorces occur according to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of 'wa' in conditional divorce is disputed: Abu Hanifa views it as sequence, the Sahibayn as simultaneity; the relied-upon position is two divorces.

The Dispute over 'Wa' in Conditional Divorce
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional divorce with 'wa'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the number of divorces that fall according to Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn when a conditional divorce uses 'wa', and explain the practical consequence of the difference.

Core Idea
With 'wa', the order of condition and pronouncement changes the count for Abu Hanifa but not for the Sahibayn.

This lesson examines a classic dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the interpretation of the conjunction 'wa' in conditional divorce utterances. The scenario: a man says to his wife, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced one and one' (or the reverse: 'You are divorced one and one if you enter the house'). How many divorces fall upon entry? Imam Abu Hanifa differentiates based on whether the condition is mentioned before or after the divorce pronouncement, while his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the Sahibayn), rule that two divorces always fall, regardless of order. We will explore the legal reasoning and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance and its structure
Consider two versions of the same utterance: one where the condition precedes the divorce pronouncement (taqdim al-shart) and one where it follows (ta'khir al-shart). The core phrase is 'You are divorced one and one' linked by 'wa'.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
According to Abu Hanifa, if the condition precedes, only one divorce falls; if the condition follows, two divorces fall. This is because the first pronouncement attaches to the condition and invalidates the second in the first case, while in the second, both attach together.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling
The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) hold that two divorces fall in both scenarios, because 'wa' indicates simultaneity (ma'iyyah), so both pronouncements attach to the condition at the same time.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if only one divorce falls (Abu Hanifa's view with condition advanced), the husband can take his wife back during the 'iddah (ruj'ah). If two divorces fall (Sahibayn's view), the divorce becomes ba'in (irrevocable), requiring a muhallil for remarriage.
5
Note the tarjih from Fath al-Qadir
The passage quotes Fath al-Qadir stating that the Sahibayn's view is stronger (arjah). Therefore, in contemporary fatwa, the reliance may be on the Sahibayn's position unless a specific need arises.

عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : يقع ثنتان فيهما ونسب لأبي حنيفة القول بأن الواو للترتيب أخذاً من قوله بوقوع الواحدة فيما إذا قدم الشرط لأنها لو كانت للجمع لتعلق الكل وليس بصحيح، بل إنما قال بالواحدة لأن هذا سلمناه ولا يفيد كالأيمان المتعاقبة، ولو سلم أن تعلق الأول علة لتعلق الثاني لم يلزم كون نزوله علة لنزوله إذ لا تلازم فجاز كونه علة لتعلقه فيتقدم في التعلق وليس نزوله علة لنزوله بل إذا تعلق الثاني بأي وتنجز الثاني ولغا الثالث، وإن أخره تنجز الأول ولغا ما بعده وقيد بحرف العطف لأنه ما ذكر بغير عطف أصلاً نحو إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق واحدة واحدة واحدة ففي فتح القدير يقع واحدة اتفاقاً عند وجود الشرط
